<p>The <b>tawny owl</b> or <b>brown owl</b> (<i>Strix aluco</i>) is a stocky, medium-sized <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owl</a> commonly found in <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodlands</a> across much of the <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a>. The tawny owl is a member of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Strix_(genus)" title="Strix (genus)">Strix</a></i>, that is also the origin of the family's name under <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_taxonomy" title="Linnaean taxonomy">Linnaean taxonomy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sclater_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sclater-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Its underparts are pale with dark streaks, and the upperparts are either rufous, brown or grey. Several of the eight recognised <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> have each of the main colour variations.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Nocturnal" class="mw-redirect" title="Nocturnal">nocturnal</a> <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">bird of prey</a> hunts a wide variety of prey species, but usually primarily takes small <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>. Tawny owls usually hunt by dropping from a perch to seize their prey, which they typically swallow whole. In more urban areas, its diet includes a higher proportion of birds, while in <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropics</a> many <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> are taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Adanez_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adanez-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Other important prey can include <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a> with other vertebrate prey taken fairly rarely.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Vision and well-developed hearing adaptations combined with silent flight aid its night hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl is capable of catching smaller owls, but is itself vulnerable to larger raptors, like <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Eagle_Owl" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian Eagle Owl">eagle-owls</a> or <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">goshawks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Koning_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koning-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> This species typically <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">nests</a>  in a <a href="/wiki/Tree_hollow" title="Tree hollow">tree hollow</a>, wherein they are likely to gain protection of their eggs and young against potential predators.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>  The tawny owl is <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">non-migratory</a> and highly territorial. Many young birds starve if they cannot find a vacant territory once parental care ceases.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti6_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti6-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Although many people believe this owl has exceptional night vision, its <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> is no more sensitive than a human's and its asymmetrically placed ears are key to its hunting by giving it excellent directional hearing. Its nocturnal habits and eerie, easily imitated call, have led to a mythical association of the tawny owl with bad luck and death.<sup id="cite_ref-Knowledge_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Dietary_biology"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Dietary biology</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Form_and_colouring">Form and colouring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Form and colouring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Owl_Yorkshire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Tawny_Owl_Yorkshire.jpg/220px-Tawny_Owl_Yorkshire.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Tawny_Owl_Yorkshire.jpg/330px-Tawny_Owl_Yorkshire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Tawny_Owl_Yorkshire.jpg/440px-Tawny_Owl_Yorkshire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="753" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Owl_Yorkshire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rufous morph individual of <i>S. a. sylvatica</i> in England.</div></div></div>
<p>This is a robust owl that is quite distinct for its large, rounded head. Tawny owls have no ear tufts but do possess a prominent facial disc rimmed in slightly dusky feathers.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Despite having a broad facial disc rim, the facial disc is largely indistinct from the surrounding feathers in markings and colour, unlike some other owls that have relative bold facial disc patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The eyes are blackish-brown rimmed (at times imperceptibly) narrowly by the pale fleshy edges of the blue-grey eyelids.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The underparts of all <a href="/wiki/Morphotype" class="mw-redirect" title="Morphotype">morphs</a> are whitish in base colour.<sup id="cite_ref-Mullarney_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mullarney-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The underside feathers are barred in a dusky colour with several crossbars, producing a <a href="/wiki/Herringbone_pattern" title="Herringbone pattern">herringbone pattern</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Their typical rich brownish colour often camouflages it well against a variety of woodland types.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls are spotted with white along the line of the scapulars, forming a white spotted "shoulder".<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The tail is rather short and the wings are broad. The tarsi and toes are densely feathered. Tawny owls possess relatively thick and heavy legs and feet and the talons are rather powerful and quite decurved.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In flight they can appear fairly big and broad, large headed and rounded on the wings.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl often flies with long glides on rounded wings, less undulating and with fewer wingbeats than other Eurasian owls, and typically at a greater height. The flight of the tawny owl is relatively heavy and slow, particularly at takeoff.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> They can appear to be a heavy flier but are capable of surprising maneuverability within woods, flying with utter silence.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> As with most owls, its flight is silent because of its feathers' soft, furry upper surfaces and a fringe on the leading edge of the outer <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather#Primaries" title="Flight feather">primaries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Neuhaus_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neuhaus-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Annual <a href="/wiki/Moulting" title="Moulting">moult</a> is usually complete in tawny owls but not all wing feathers are moulted each year.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Feathers are moulted gradually between June and December.<sup id="cite_ref-RSPB_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSPB-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Of 91 males and 214 females in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, 17-19% did not moult any primaries, while 1-6% replaced all primaries, about 6% of males and 2% of females annually replaced median-primaries, while about 11% of males and 4% of females replaced annually their median secondaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Petty_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Young individuals can sometimes be diagnosed to age roughly by the state of wing moult. However, some variation in wing moult accounts for mistaken identification by age of 3 year old owls for younger ages due to the fact that they retain some worn juvenile wing feathers. Moult tends to occur after young fledge in late summer-early autumn for mature owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Petty2_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty2-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_Beachy_Head.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Strix_aluco_Beachy_Head.jpg/220px-Strix_aluco_Beachy_Head.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Strix_aluco_Beachy_Head.jpg/330px-Strix_aluco_Beachy_Head.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Strix_aluco_Beachy_Head.jpg/440px-Strix_aluco_Beachy_Head.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_Beachy_Head.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny owl awoken during the day.</div></div></div>
<p>Plumage coloring in this species can be very variable. The <a href="/wiki/Subspecies#Nomenclature" title="Subspecies">nominate race</a> in particular has two main <a href="/wiki/Morphotype" class="mw-redirect" title="Morphotype">morphs</a> which differ in their <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> colour. The predominant morphs are grey and rufous, with a more minor intermediate or brown morph also sometimes occurring in various races; sometimes each morph may intergrade.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The plumage colour is <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genetically</a> controlled. There is some indication that <a href="/wiki/Pleiotropy" title="Pleiotropy">pleiotropy</a> has led to the colouring diversity in the species.<sup id="cite_ref-Emaresi_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emaresi-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Studies, largely based in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, based on contour feathers indicate that grey morph owls are more densely insulated and better suited to surviving cooler conditions, which is roughly in keeping with the respective morph distribution. Meanwhile, in warmer and wetter and/or more humid conditions, rufous morph individuals are better adapted.<sup id="cite_ref-Emaresi_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emaresi-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> However, similar studies on climate, habitat and colour morph found no strong correlation between colour morph, habitat and survivorship in <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Studies on colour morphs also indicated that higher levels of <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanin</a>, such as darker rufous morphs, may suffer higher rates of parasitism, body mass loss through the season across all ages but on the contrary also had higher growth rates for nestlings and were more likely to breed every year than grey morphs in Italy and Switzerland regardless of prey resources than grey morphs.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Emaresi_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emaresi-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Studies in Finland indicate that grey morph tawny owls have more reproductive success, better immune resistance, and fewer <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasites</a> than other morphs. The data on grey morphs having the aforementioned advantages is also supported in Italian data.<sup id="cite_ref-Emaresi_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emaresi-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Koskenpato_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koskenpato-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Emaresi2_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emaresi2-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Although this might suggest that eventually the darker morphs could disappear, the owls show no colour preference when choosing a mate, so the <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_pressure" title="Evolutionary pressure">selection pressure</a> in favour of the grey morph is reduced. There are also environmental factors involved. The Italian study showed that brown-morph birds were found in denser woodland, and in Finland, <a href="/wiki/Gloger%27s_rule" title="Gloger&#39;s rule">Gloger's rule</a> would suggest that paler birds would in any case predominate in the colder climate.<sup id="cite_ref-Brommer_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brommer-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Parasitaemia_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parasitaemia-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, neither primary morph was necessary predominant, with 51.4% of 107 owls being rufous morph and 46.7% being grey morphs and this may qualify as a transitional zone.<sup id="cite_ref-Gryz_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gryz-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Other areas studied for colour morph proportions showed the following, in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> (sample size 31): 55% rufous 39% grey and 6% intermediate; in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> (315): 65% rufous and 35% grey; in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> (54): 26% rufous, 65% grey and 9% intermediate; in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> (50): 10% rufous and 90% grey; in <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> (102): 32.3% rufous, 61.8% grey and 5.9% intermediate; <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> (79): 33% rufous and 67% grey.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_qtl1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Strix_aluco_qtl1.jpg/220px-Strix_aluco_qtl1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Strix_aluco_qtl1.jpg/330px-Strix_aluco_qtl1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Strix_aluco_qtl1.jpg/440px-Strix_aluco_qtl1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2980" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_qtl1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A female and male pair in captivity to illustrate the sexual dimorphism by size.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny owl is a medium-sized species of owl. This species is <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexually dimorphic</a>; the female is notably larger than the male, often averaging up to 5% longer and can average more than 25% heavier.<sup id="cite_ref-BTO_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BTO-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> This is sometimes called reverse sexual dimorphism (RSD) as it runs opposite of most birds, wherein males are usually larger, but almost all unrelated groups of <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a> display some degree of RSD.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Of European owls, the tawny owl ranks as fourth most dimorphic by weight and fifth most dimorphic by wing dimensions. The prevalent <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesis</a> is that RSD occurs in birds of prey due to the considerable rigors of the breeding cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Of the <i>Strix</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>  it is perhaps the smallest.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Total length in the tawny owl ranges from 36 to 46&#160;cm (14 to 18&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Average length in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> was found to be 36.7&#160;cm (14.4&#160;in) in 10 males and 37.7&#160;cm (14.8&#160;in) in 18 females.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Average total length in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> was 38.9&#160;cm (15.3&#160;in) in 10 males and 39.3&#160;cm (15.5&#160;in) in 12 females.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Wingspan may vary from 81 to 105&#160;cm (32 to 41&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> In Denmark, the average wingspan in 9 males was 89.7&#160;cm (35.3&#160;in) and in 12 females was 91.9&#160;cm (36.2&#160;in) and in Spain the average was 87&#160;cm (34&#160;in) in 14 males and 88.7&#160;cm (34.9&#160;in) in 12 females.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Among standard measurements, across the various subspecies, the <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> of males can range from 248 to 323&#160;mm (9.8 to 12.7&#160;in) whilst that of the female may vary from 255 to 343&#160;mm (10.0 to 13.5&#160;in). <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">Tail</a> length can vary from 148 to 210&#160;mm (5.8 to 8.3&#160;in) while the less widely measured linear variants of <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">total bill</a> length have been reported at 28 to 35&#160;mm (1.1 to 1.4&#160;in) and <a href="/wiki/Tibiotarsus" title="Tibiotarsus">tarsus</a> at 45 to 63&#160;mm (1.8 to 2.5&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karel_Voous" title="Karel Voous">Voous</a> claimed a mean weight of 474&#160;g (1.045&#160;lb) for males and 583&#160;g (1.285&#160;lb), which would render them 70% more massive than an average <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio otus</i>) and 60% more massive than an average <a href="/wiki/Western_barn_owl" title="Western barn owl">western barn owl</a> (<i>Tyto alba</i>) despite these species' similar appearance by size.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In Denmark, males and females were found to vary in weight from 392 to 692&#160;g (0.864 to 1.526&#160;lb), with an average weight across seasons was 490&#160;g (1.08&#160;lb) for both sexes, or 440&#160;g (16&#160;oz) for males and 539.7&#160;g (1.190&#160;lb) for females. In Danish owls, weights were lowest during brooding and fledgling stages and highest in winter, varying up to 12% and 10%, respectively, in males and females.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Weight fluctuations by season were even more pronounced in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, where the mean weights of males and females in winter and late spring differed by 17% in males and nearly 20% in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Baudvin_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baudvin-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In southern <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> body mass were studied instead by age, with the division of 3 years considered for 172 females and 135 males. In the males, weight was nearly the same across the age divide, being 481.6&#160;g (1.062&#160;lb) in the younger males and 480.2&#160;g (1.059&#160;lb) in the older ones, however older females were notably larger than young ones, with the younger females averaging 689.1&#160;g (1.519&#160;lb) and older ones averaging 731.6&#160;g (1.613&#160;lb). Older females in the Finnish study were found on average to also breed earlier, be more productive and adapt better to varying prey conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Solonen_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solonen-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, the weights of freshly dead owls was studied against live ones weighed in the wild, with 79 dead females averaging 484.5&#160;g (1.068&#160;lb) against an average of 533&#160;g (1.175&#160;lb) for 22 live females. Meanwhile, in males 384.1&#160;g (13.55&#160;oz) was found to be the average for 63 dead individuals, while 20 live ones averaged 408.6&#160;g (14.41&#160;oz). It was found in English and Scottish tawny owls that the weight down to which males and females could survive starvation could go as low as 325&#160;g (11.5&#160;oz) in males and 390&#160;g (14&#160;oz) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Hardy_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hardy-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the average weight of  16 males was 406.2&#160;g (14.33&#160;oz) and for 19 females was 460&#160;g (1.01&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In total, weight can vary from 304 to 800&#160;g (0.670 to 1.764&#160;lb) in full grown tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hardy_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hardy-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_1_(Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Strix_aluco_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/220px-Strix_aluco_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Strix_aluco_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/330px-Strix_aluco_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Strix_aluco_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/440px-Strix_aluco_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_1_(Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny owl in the Czech Republic.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hearing_and_auditory_morphology">Hearing and auditory morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hearing and auditory morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hearing is important for a nocturnal <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">bird of prey</a>, and as with other owls, the tawny owl's two ear openings differ in structure and are asymmetrically placed to improve directional hearing. A passage through the skull links the eardrums, and small differences in the time of arrival of a sound at each ear enables its source to be pinpointed. The left ear opening is higher on the head than the larger right ear and tilts downward, improving sensitivity to sounds from below.<sup id="cite_ref-Burton_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> While the species does show the typical ear <a href="/wiki/Asymmetry" title="Asymmetry">asymmetry</a> of an owl and the right ear is consistently larger, the average differences of 7-13% are relatively modest for an owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Norberg_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norberg-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Both ear openings are hidden under the facial disk feathers, which are structurally specialized to be transparent to sound, and are supported by a movable fold of skin (the pre-aural flap).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The ear slits average reportedly 21 to 23&#160;mm (0.83 to 0.91&#160;in) on the left and 22.5 to 26&#160;mm (0.89 to 1.02&#160;in) on the right.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous2_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous2-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Norberg_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norberg-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The movable pre-aural skin flap on tawny owl averages 9.5&#160;mm (0.37&#160;in) on the left and 10.5&#160;mm (0.41&#160;in) on the right.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous2_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous2-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Norberg_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norberg-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owls has a comparable ear morphology to the <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owl</a> (<i>Strix uralensis</i>). They tend to have a less complicated ear structure than those of <i><a href="/wiki/Asio" title="Asio">Asio</a></i> species but a more complicated, well-developed and relatively larger ear structure than those of other large generas of typical owl like the <i><a href="/wiki/Horned_owl" title="Horned owl">Bubo</a></i> genus or <i><a href="/wiki/Scops_owl" title="Scops owl">Otus</a></i> genus.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Norberg_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norberg-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The internal structure of the ear, which has large numbers of auditory <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>, gives an improved ability to detect low-frequency sounds at a distance, which could include rustling made by prey moving in vegetation.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl's hearing may be ten times better than a human's,<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and it can hunt using this sense alone in the dark of a woodland on an overcast night. However, the patter of raindrops can make it likely difficult for these owls to detect faint sounds, and prolonged wet weather, especially the crashing din of heavy rain, can lead to starvation if the owl cannot hunt effectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Burton_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The tawny's range is estimated to average 0.4-0.7 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">kHz</a> with a maximum of around 3&#160;kHz.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous2_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous2-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The maximum range, in comparison, is up to 6&#160;kHz in the <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> and to 1&#160;kHz in the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">eagle owl</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalizations">Vocalizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Vocalizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="61.89820861678" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Tawny_Owl_(Strix_aluco)_(W1CDR0001519_BD8).ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/91/Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_%28W1CDR0001519_BD8%29.ogg/Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_%28W1CDR0001519_BD8%29.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="106184" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_%28W1CDR0001519_BD8%29.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (186 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="185661" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Owl_(Strix_aluco)_(W1CDR0001519_BD8).ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hooting song, Gloucestershire, England, 1978</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="49.72514739229" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Tawny_Owl_(Strix_aluco)_(W1CDR0001427_BD9).ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c0/Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_%28W1CDR0001427_BD9%29.ogg/Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_%28W1CDR0001427_BD9%29.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="130760" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_%28W1CDR0001427_BD9%29.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (212 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="212117" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Owl_(Strix_aluco)_(W1CDR0001427_BD9).ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>'Kewick' calls, England, 1960s</div></div></div>
<p>Advertising calls and most threat and supplanting calls are mostly by males while both sexes may engage in contact calls and alarm calls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Autumn boundary disputes may occur with excited varied wails and screams between hoots (or "caterwauling").<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The male has a quavering advertising song <i>hoo...ho, ho, hoo-hoo-hoo-hoo</i> or <i>whooooh uk whooooook</i>. It is described as "a clear, fluted, long-drawn hoot with a wailing quality".<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Broken down, the male's song is considered as about three notes drawn together into one, often with an upward inflection and emphasis on the middle note, followed after a brief pause followed by a very short <i>ho</i>, <i>uk</i> or <i>hu</i> and continuing after a further short interval with a long <a href="/wiki/Tremolo" title="Tremolo">tremolo</a> of <a href="/wiki/Staccato" title="Staccato">staccato</a> notes, often rising or falling slightly in pitch and drawn out in the end. On average, the male's song is about 17 seconds in duration.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti2_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti2-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The song may carry up to 1.5 to 2&#160;km (0.93 to 1.24&#160;mi) to human perception.<sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> More than 99% of the time, it was found that individual males could be distinguished via <a href="/wiki/Spectrogram" title="Spectrogram">spectrogram</a> in Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti2_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti2-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> A female territorial call is somewhat like the male's but is hoarser, less clear and somewhat higher in pitch, transcribed as <i>cher oooOOooo</i> followed by <i>chro cher-oooOOooo cooEEooooo</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> used this owl's calls in <i><a href="/wiki/Love%27s_Labour%27s_Lost" title="Love&#39;s Labour&#39;s Lost">Love's Labour's Lost</a></i> (Act 5, Scene 2) as "Then nightly sings the staring owl, Tu-whit; Tu-who, a merry note, While greasy Joan doth keel the pot", but this <a href="/wiki/Stereotype" title="Stereotype">stereotypical</a> call is actually a duet, with the female making the <i>kew-wick</i> contact call.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mullarney_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mullarney-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The male's response to the females <i>kewick</i> contact call is more varied, sometimes muffled and fluting notes, sometimes wavering or crooning notes and sometimes a more dissimilar hissing <i>chruuuuuu</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Southern_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hirons_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>  The calls of tawny owls are easily imitated by blowing into cupped hands through slightly parted thumbs, and a study in <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire" title="Cambridgeshire">Cambridgeshire</a> found that this <a href="/wiki/Mimicry" title="Mimicry">mimicry</a> produced a response from the owl within 30&#160;minutes in 94% of trials.<sup id="cite_ref-redpath94_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-redpath94-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Recordings of various calls may also be an effective way for researchers to study territories and owl responsiveness. English male tawny owls were responsive both male and female calls, the latter perhaps due to an interest for mates, while females usually only responded to recordings of female calls.<sup id="cite_ref-Appleby_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appleby-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sleepy_Owl_(5500098793).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sleepy_Owl_%285500098793%29.jpg/220px-Sleepy_Owl_%285500098793%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="316" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sleepy_Owl_%285500098793%29.jpg/330px-Sleepy_Owl_%285500098793%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sleepy_Owl_%285500098793%29.jpg/440px-Sleepy_Owl_%285500098793%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="791" data-file-height="1135" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sleepy_Owl_(5500098793).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A drowsy tawny owl during the day</div></div></div>
<p>Song posts are often only 250 to 300&#160;m (820 to 980&#160;ft) from their roost sites.<sup id="cite_ref-Southern_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hirons_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In an <a href="/wiki/Pavia" title="Pavia">Italian</a> study, 12 males responded much more strongly to recordings of "stranger" male tawnys than to recordings of neighboring male owls known to them, in some cases coming to physically attack the recorder when the "stranger" call was playing.<sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti3_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti3-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> A study within <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> recording only spontaneous vocalizations that only a low percentage of territories could be detected this way, about 12%, and that males spontaneously called about 2 to 4 times more frequently than females.<sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> A male's response to a broadcast song appears to be indicative of his health and vigour; owls with higher blood parasite loads use fewer high frequencies and a more limited range of frequencies in their responses to an apparent intruder.<sup id="cite_ref-Redpath_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redpath-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In Italy, males hooted more emphatically when the female was in the vicinity.<sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti4_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti4-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The vocal activity of tawny owls depends on sex, annual cycle stage and weather, with males being more vocal than females year-round, with peak vocal activity during courtship, late winter to early spring, and post-breeding in early autumn, the two times of year when territories are most hotly contested. The least frequent vocalizing as a whole being is in Decemberâ€“February and during mid-May to early September, but most especially June to July.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Males in particular may call even at the quietest times of year, i.e. usually when he is presumably excited or irritated.<sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Female territorial hooting is almost entirely confined to autumn.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In Italy, females were more aggressive on average than males in response to playback despite lower response levels and exhibited much higher aggression when both members of the pair were present.<sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti5_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti5-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> A correlation was also made in the amount of singing relative to habitat, with owls with territories in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">farmland</a> responding more vigorously to imitated calls than those in <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-redpath94_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-redpath94-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> More moonlit nights showed more aggressive vocal displays in <a href="/wiki/Pavia" title="Pavia">Pavia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> despite other seasonal and temporal factors seeming to play no role.<sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti4_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti4-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, studied tawny owls vocalized significantly less during rainy nights, with a very strong 8 fold difference in <a href="/wiki/Psychophysics" title="Psychophysics">discrimination threshold</a> (carrying 614 to 74&#160;m (2,014 to 243&#160;ft)) and 69 fold difference in audible broadcast area (118.4 to 1.7&#160;ha (292.6 to 4.2 acres)) in dry against rainy weather.<sup id="cite_ref-Lengagne_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lengagne-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In 50 British tawny owls that were studied in terms of singing duration and quality no correlation was made between breeding success and amount of singing, though larger males sang less but with more emphasis on the last note while those with more evidence of parasites sang more on average.<sup id="cite_ref-Appleby2_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appleby2-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> A study of the two main European races, one based in Italy (<i>S. a. aluco</i>) and one in England (<i>S. a. sylvatica</i>), showed the primary song of the male differed significantly in five of the 13 parameters considered, duration of the second note, lowest frequency of the first note, and frequency modulation amplitude being the most important variables and the two races could be aurally discriminated with a high success rate (86.7%). Variation was also found within the races by habitat especially. A patchwork pattern of possibly culturally transmitted hoot variations was therefore recognized, which may indicate tawny owls have <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoot_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoot-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The calls of the subspecies in <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeastern Europe">southeastern Europe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> (<i>S. a. willkonskii</i>) similarly differed in half of  the six parameters considered from the nominate subspecies, with the singing having an overall lower <a href="/wiki/Tonality" title="Tonality">tone</a> in keeping with the race's slightly larger size.<sup id="cite_ref-Shekhovtsov_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shekhovtsov-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Further described calls by tawny owls include piercing <i>coo-wik</i> or <i>cu-weeehl</i> cries, apparently expressing aggression. When disturbed at the nest, these owls may utter a series of yelping <i>uett-uett-uett</i>.. notes.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Boundary disputes frequently cause males to utter a <i>wett wett, weck weck weck</i> or <i>gweck gweck gweck</i> call, while females do a significantly less deep-sounding version of this. Females may also engage in a similar vocalization before launching a protective attack against predators.<sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> A further bubbling call, uttered by both sexes, the males' version being softer and lower, the females being harsher, liken to human uttering <i>ooo</i> sound while moving tongue back and forth, due to the soft rolling sound, but also has been compared to the drumming sound of a <a href="/wiki/Common_snipe" title="Common snipe">common snipe</a> (<i>Gallinago gallinago</i>). Although fairly frequent during high intensity pair interactions (such as during nest inspections), the bubbling call is difficult to detect except at close range so is probably underreported.<sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Spencer_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spencer-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pig</a>-like grunting has sometimes been reported as emitted by males during courtship displays.<sup id="cite_ref-Scherzinger_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scherzinger-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Leading up to food deliveries by the male, the female may utter a food excitement call, <i>kiv-kiv-kiv-kiv</i>..., culminating in a peeping whistling <i>sii-sii-si-siiiii</i> as she receives prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Other calls uttered by females <i>witt-witt</i> (before copulation) and rapid <i>kikikikiiii</i> (during copulation), both of which may be the same call, while a high-pitched, trilling sound similar to <a href="/wiki/European_green_toad" title="European green toad">European green toad</a> (<i>Bufo virdis</i>), sometimes transcribed as <i>lee-lee-lee</i>, is uttered by the female in similar contexts.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The female may call <i>hung-hung</i> or <i>ung-ung-ung-haug-haug</i> when comforting their nestlings or trying to get reluctant young to eat.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stulcken_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stulcken-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> A distraction display call may be engaged in by either parent, wavering <i>iiiii</i> or <i>keeee</i>, similar to the chipping of a <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerine</a>, has been described as "unsteady flute-like piping of despair".<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Males may utter a <i>chochochocho</i>, apparently to express gentleness when in close quarters with its mate.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> A mysterious call described as the long call is of unknown meaning, long call, consisting of a moaning, elongated note of unknown meaning, often isolated from any other kind of vocalization and reminiscent of the mewing of a <a href="/wiki/European_herring_gull" title="European herring gull">herring gull</a> (<i>Larus argentatus</i>), <i>keeeee keeuuuh keeuhkuhkuh</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Other mysterious calls recorded for tawny owls have included tooting, chittering, crowing, screeching or mewing, as well as soft, plaintive squeaks by females.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The young owls in nest beg for food with a drawn-out <i>cheek</i> or <i>cheheee</i>, <i>sziii-szi</i>, <i>psji-ii</i> or <i>tsjuk</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Delicate, piping <i>pipipipi</i> calls may uttered by nestlings in discomfort (often recorded when the mother interrupts brooding).<sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> From when they can actively feed themselves to when they are fledged, the young draw out their call to a wheezy, louder and more boisterous <i>tsi-weep</i>, being less highed and squeaky than the begging call of the long-eared owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> By the first year, the young tawny owls have an adult voice but it is usually slightly higher pitched.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vision">Vision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Vision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Owlretina.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Owlretina.svg/220px-Owlretina.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Owlretina.svg/330px-Owlretina.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Owlretina.svg/440px-Owlretina.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="850" data-file-height="615" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Owlretina.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An owl's retina has a single fovea.<sup id="cite_ref-Sturkie_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sturkie-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>An owl's eyes are placed at the front of the head and have a field overlap of 50â€“70%, giving it better <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">binocular vision</a> than diurnal birds of prey (overlap 30â€“50%).<sup id="cite_ref-Burton_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brooke_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brooke-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The diameter of the eye reaches about 16 to 23&#160;mm (0.63 to 0.91&#160;in), against 11&#160;mm (0.43&#160;in) in the long-eared owl, while the tawny's axial length reaches 29 to 35.7&#160;mm (1.14 to 1.41&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brooke_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brooke-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl's <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> has about 56,000 light-sensitive <a href="/wiki/Rod_cell" title="Rod cell">rod cells</a> per square millimetre (36&#160;million per square inch); although earlier claims that it could see in the <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> part of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">spectrum</a> have been dismissed,<sup id="cite_ref-Hecht_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hecht-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> it is still often said to have <a href="/wiki/Bird_vision" title="Bird vision">eyesight</a> 10 to 100 times better than humans in low-light conditions. However, the experimental basis for this claim is probably inaccurate by at least a factor of 10.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The owl's actual visual acuity is only slightly greater than that of humans, and any increased sensitivity is due to optical factors rather than to greater retinal sensitivity; both humans and owl have reached the limit of resolution for the retinas of terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin2_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In addition to middling visual acuity relative to other vertebrates, the <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">colour discrimination</a> in the vision of this owl may be limited.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferens_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferens-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bowmaker_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowmaker-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fieldofview-pigeon-owl.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Fieldofview-pigeon-owl.svg/220px-Fieldofview-pigeon-owl.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Fieldofview-pigeon-owl.svg/330px-Fieldofview-pigeon-owl.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Fieldofview-pigeon-owl.svg/440px-Fieldofview-pigeon-owl.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fieldofview-pigeon-owl.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Field of view compared with a pigeon</div></div></div>
<p>Adaptations to night vision include the large size of the eye, its tubular shape, large numbers of closely packed retinal rods, and an absence of <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">cone cells</a>, since <a href="/wiki/Rod_cell" title="Rod cell">rod cells</a> have superior light sensitivity. There are few coloured oil drops, which would reduce the light intensity.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Unlike diurnal birds of prey, owls normally have only one <a href="/wiki/Fovea_centralis" title="Fovea centralis">fovea</a>, and that is poorly developed except in daytime and dusk hunters like the <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Burton_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> When compared to a diurnal bird like a <a href="/wiki/Rock_dove" title="Rock dove">rock dove</a> (<i>Columba livia</i>), the tawny owl's ability to navigate at night does not appear to lie in its light gather power or number of rod receptors but instead is due to the differences in the retinal <a href="/wiki/Functional_integration_(neurobiology)" title="Functional integration (neurobiology)">neural integration mechanisms</a> which is only possible due to the absolutely large size of the rental image only because of its absolutely large sized retinal image.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin3_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martin4_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin4-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> It was hypothesized that the tawny owls ability to navigate their surroundings may be correlated in part due to familiarity with the environment of their territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin5_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin5-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Confusion_species">Confusion species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Confusion species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tawny_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg/220px-Tawny_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tawny_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg/330px-Tawny_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tawny_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg/440px-Tawny_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2550" data-file-height="3300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A composite image of the western subspecies <i>S. a. sylvatica</i>, for identification.</div></div></div>
<p>Generally speaking, the tawny owl is unlikely to be mistaken for other owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Crossley_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crossley-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Its size, squat shape and broad wings distinguish it from other owls found within its range; other <i><a href="/wiki/Strix_(genus)" title="Strix (genus)">Strix</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">eagle-owls</a> can be somewhat similar in shape, but are much larger.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Despite two other European species being included in the <i>Strix</i> genus, the tawny is rather distinct from the others.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owl</a> is notably larger with proportionately smaller dark brown eyes, a proportionately smaller head and the underparts are streaked without crossbars. Furthermore, Ural owls have a proportionately longer and more distinctly barred tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Great_grey_owl" title="Great grey owl">great grey owl</a> (<i>Strix nebulosa</i>) is much larger than a tawny owl with a huge head, a proportionately longer tail, a more uniform dark-greyish plumage, a darker facial disc with distinct concentric lines and relatively smaller yellow eyes.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Desert_owl" title="Desert owl">desert or Hume's owls</a> (<i>Strix hadorami</i>) are almost entirely <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">allopatric</a> in distribution from tawny owls. Both species range into some mutual areas, such as the northern <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> including <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> but almost always in different areas and habitats. The Hume's owl is smaller than the tawny owl and more sandy-colored with orange-yellow to pale ochraceous-orange eyes.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Among owls of the <i><a href="/wiki/Asio" title="Asio">Asio</a></i> genus, all species are smaller to a certain degree and quite different looking. All <i>Asio</i> species also prefer more open habitats than tawny owls as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marsh_owl" title="Marsh owl">Marsh owl</a> (<i>Asio capensis</i>) which overlaps scarcely in <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwestern Africa">Northwestern Africa</a> has smaller ear tufts and is rather uniform earthen-brown above with pale facial disc. <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">Short-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>) has smaller ear tufts, yellow eyes and streaked underparts. The <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio otus</i>) is slighter in build and weight than a tawny owl with prominent ear tufts, orange eyes and different plumage patterning.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> All <a href="/wiki/Horned_owl" title="Horned owl">eagle-owls and fish-owls</a> are much larger than tawny owls with prominent ear tufts and distinct plumages.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_subspecies">Taxonomy and subspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and subspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The species was first described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his landmark 1758 <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition of <i>Systema Naturae</i></a> under its current scientific name.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial</a> derives from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>strix</i> "owl" and <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> <i>allocco</i>, "tawny owl" (from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>ulucus</i> "screech-owl").<sup id="cite_ref-BTO_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BTO-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Some early descriptions upon review were found to have somehow <a href="/wiki/Conflation" title="Conflation">conflated</a> the very different <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a> with the scientific name <i>Strix aluco</i>, which in turn engendered some confusion.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TawnyOwlBeingPointedAt.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/TawnyOwlBeingPointedAt.png/170px-TawnyOwlBeingPointedAt.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/TawnyOwlBeingPointedAt.png/255px-TawnyOwlBeingPointedAt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/TawnyOwlBeingPointedAt.png/340px-TawnyOwlBeingPointedAt.png 2x" data-file-width="364" data-file-height="458" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TawnyOwlBeingPointedAt.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An individual from France perched upon a human hand</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny owl is a member of the wood-owl genus <i><a href="/wiki/Strix_(genus)" title="Strix (genus)">Strix</a></i>, part of the <a href="/wiki/True_owl" title="True owl">typical owl</a> family Strigidae, which contains all species of owl other than the <a href="/wiki/Tytonidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Tytonidae">barn owls</a>. Conservatively, about 18 species are currently represented in this <i>Strix</i> genus, typically being medium to large sized owls, characteristically round-headed and lacking ear tufts, which acclimate to living in forested parts of various climatic zones.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sibley_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sibley-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Four owls native to the <a href="/wiki/Neotropical_realm" title="Neotropical realm">neotropics</a> are sometimes additionally included with the <i>Strix</i> genus though some authors include these in a separate but related genus, <i><a href="/wiki/Ciccaba" title="Ciccaba">Ciccaba</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ioc_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ioc-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl is smallish for a <i>Strix</i> species, the smallest of the <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_distribution" title="Circumpolar distribution">circumpolar</a> species, mildly larger than the species from the <a href="/wiki/Neotropical_realm" title="Neotropical realm">neotropics</a> and tropical species from <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, and slightly to considerably smaller than the Asian tropical species.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <i>Strix</i> owls have an extensive fossil record and have long been widely distributed.<sup id="cite_ref-MlÃ­kovsky_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MlÃ­kovsky-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The genetic relationship of <a href="/wiki/True_owl" title="True owl">true owls</a> is somewhat muddled and different genetic testings has variously indicated that <i>Strix</i> owls are related to disparate appearing genera like <i><a href="/wiki/Pulsatrix" title="Pulsatrix">Pulsatrix</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Horned_owl" title="Horned owl">Bubo</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Asio" title="Asio">Asio</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lee_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Tropical species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mottled_owl" title="Mottled owl">mottled owl</a> (<i>Strix virgata</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/African_wood_owl" title="African wood owl">African wood owl</a> (<i>Strix woodfordii</i>), the latter once considered a close relative to the tawny owl, morphological differ from and have smaller outer ear areas than tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The tawny owl is thought to be a close relative of the <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owl</a>. Authors have hypothesized that the origin of the two species divided following <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">continental glaciations</a> segregated a southwest or southern group in <a href="/wiki/Temperate_forest" title="Temperate forest">temperate forest</a> (i.e. the tawny) from an eastern one inhabiting cold, <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">boreal ranges</a> (i.e. the Ural). After retreat of the continental ice masses, the ranges more recently penetrated each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous2_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous2-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Salomonsen_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salomonsen-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> While the life history details of the tawny and Ural owls are largely corresponding, nonetheless the species have a number of <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> differences and are largely adapted to different <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climates</a>, times of activity and habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scherzinger_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scherzinger-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Based on <i>Strix</i> fossil species from <a href="/wiki/Middle_Pleistocene" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Pleistocene">Middle Pleistocene</a> (given the name <i>Strix intermedia</i>) in variously the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> show from leg and wing bones indicate an owl of intermediate form and size between Ural and tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Janossy_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janossy-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Janossy2_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janossy2-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Janossy3_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janossy3-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mourer-Chauvire_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mourer-Chauvire-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> However, fossils of a larger and differently proportioned <i>Strix</i> owl than a tawny owl, identified as <i>Strix brevis</i>, from Germany and Hungary from before the Pleistocene (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Piacenzian" title="Piacenzian">Piacenzian</a>) suggest a more complicated evolutionary and distributional history.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Janossy2_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janossy2-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Janossy3_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janossy3-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mourer-Chauvire_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mourer-Chauvire-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Becker_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> was recorded in captivity between a male Ural and a female tawny owl, which managed to produce two offspring that were intermediate in size and had a more complex song that was also shared some characteristics with both species' vocalizations.<sup id="cite_ref-Hybrid_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hybrid-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of owls that were considered <a href="/wiki/Biological_specificity" title="Biological specificity">conspecific</a> with the tawny owl are now widely considered to be separate species via conclusive genetic studies. These consist of the <a href="/wiki/Desert_owl" title="Desert owl">desert or Hume's owl</a> and its sister species, the recently separated and range-restricted <a href="/wiki/Omani_owl" title="Omani owl">Omani owl</a> (<i>Strix butleri</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Heidrich_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heidrich-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kirwan_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirwan-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Robb_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robb-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Another species even more recently found to be distinct from tawny owls is the little-known <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_owl" title="Himalayan owl">Himalayan owl</a> (<i>Strix nivicolum</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-delHoyo_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-delHoyo-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In all three separated species there is no evidence that the tawny owl breeds in the same areas as them, making each species <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">allopatric</a>, though the desert and tawny's range nearly abuts in some parts of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> such as northern <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kirwan_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirwan-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dov_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dov-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Also, in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Himalayas" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Himalayas">Western Himalayas</a> both the tawny and Himalayan owls are known to occur but there is likely a gap of several hundred kilometers in distribution with tawny mostly restricted to the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistani</a> side while the Himalayan is rarely found west of <a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marcot_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marcot-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the desert and Omani species pair and the Himalayan species are considerably different based on superficial appearance (far more so than true tawny owl subspecies), have distinct voices and appear to have slightly different nesting habits than tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The tawny owl subspecies are often poorly differentiated, and may be at a flexible stage of subspecies formation with features related to the ambient temperature, the colour tone of the local habitat, and the size of available prey. Consequently, various authors have historically described between 10 and 15 subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The total number of subspecies was once considered as totaling at 11 subspecies but is now reduced due to the separation of the Himalayan owl and its own further two subspecies down to about eight subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The currently recognised subspecies are listed below.<sup id="cite_ref-WOT_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WOT-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table width="67%" class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th width="15%">Subspecies
</th>
<th width="25%">Range
</th>
<th width="17%">Described by <small>(parentheses indicate originally in a different genus)</small>
</th>
<th width="25%">Description
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><i>S. a. aluco</i>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">North</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a> from southern <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a>
</td>
<td>Linnaeus, 1758
</td>
<td>Markedly <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">polymorphic</a> with all three morphs known. Some brown morph individuals bear indistinct concentric lines on the facial disc and tend to have a disc rim is dark brown.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Generally, birds of the nominate subspecies are rather pale below with sparser markings and more <a href="/wiki/Cream" title="Cream">creamy</a> base colour showing than other European tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Study of genetic <a href="/wiki/Phylogeography" title="Phylogeography">phylogeography</a> of showed that the population of the nominate race in the <a href="/wiki/Balkan" class="mw-redirect" title="Balkan">Balkans</a> originated as a postglacial occupation of northern territories, although these populations do interbreed with populations of two other clines, in the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> This is a medium-sized subspecies. In <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> males may measure 259 to 286&#160;mm (10.2 to 11.3&#160;in) and females may measure 268 to 298&#160;mm (10.6 to 11.7&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> measures 148 to 166&#160;mm (5.8 to 6.5&#160;in) in males and 154 to 171&#160;mm (6.1 to 6.7&#160;in) in females.
