In both sexes, the tarsus may measure 45 to 53&#160;mm (1.8 to 2.1&#160;in) and the bill 28.5 to 34.5&#160;mm (1.12 to 1.36&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Unlike the species overall, the nominate subspecies neatly corresponds to <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_rule" title="Bergmann&#39;s rule">Bergmann's rule</a> (wherein animal are larger farther from the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>). In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Italy" title="Northern Italy">northern Italy</a>, the average wing chord in males and females, respectively, was 267 and 274.5&#160;mm (10.51 and 10.81&#160;in) and body mass averaged 445 and 543&#160;g (0.981 and 1.197&#160;lb) in the two sexes.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Much farther north in Finland, nominate race owls were notably larger, averaging 275 and 287&#160;mm (10.8 and 11.3&#160;in) in wing chord and 480 and 699&#160;g (1.058 and 1.541&#160;lb) in body mass.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Solonen_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solonen-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Taken as a whole, the nominate includes both the heavier and lightest recorded birds in the tawny owl species.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Solonen_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solonen-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i>S. a. sylvatica</i>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">West Europe</a> including <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/George_Shaw" title="George Shaw">Shaw</a>, 1809
</td>
<td>Generally the appearance of <i>S. a. sylvatica</i> is not dissimilar from the nominate subspecies but on average it is more boldly patterned with considerably less white base colour showing below, particularly with a richer average hue in rufous and intermediate morph individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> More significantly, the main song of this subspecies differs slightly from that of nominate subspecies based on spectrograms.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoot_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoot-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Linearly, this is a fairly small subspecies, averaging around 10% smaller than <i>S. a. aluco</i>, and may include the smallest known tawny owls going on standard measurements.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> However, average weights do not significantly differ from those of other subspecies with published weights.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">Wing chord</a> measurements may range from 248 to 280&#160;mm (9.8 to 11.0&#160;in) in males and from 255 to 296&#160;mm (10.0 to 11.7&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baudvin_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baudvin-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In Spain, the tail could measure 140 to 191&#160;mm (5.5 to 7.5&#160;in), averaging 167.8&#160;mm (6.61&#160;in), the tarsus could measure 47 to 61&#160;mm (1.9 to 2.4&#160;in), averaging 53.85&#160;mm (2.120&#160;in) and the bill could measure 24 to 31&#160;mm (0.94 to 1.22&#160;in), averaging 28.5&#160;mm (1.12&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_31-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Average wing chord in males from England and France were 260.9 and 268&#160;mm (10.27 and 10.55&#160;in) respectively while those of females were 273.6 and 276&#160;mm (10.77 and 10.87&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Hardy_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hardy-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baudvin_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baudvin-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Average weights in England and France were 408.6 and 427.8&#160;g (14.41 and 15.09&#160;oz) for 22 and 66 males and 533 and 567&#160;g (1.175 and 1.250&#160;lb) in 20 and 50 females.<sup id="cite_ref-Hardy_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hardy-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baudvin_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baudvin-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Live adult weights can range from 335 to 580&#160;g (0.739 to 1.279&#160;lb) in males and 430 to 780&#160;g (0.95 to 1.72&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Baudvin_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baudvin-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i>S. a. biddulphi</i>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">NW India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>
</td>
<td>Scully, 1881<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</td>
<td>This isolated subspecies is fairly distinct for its stark grey morph, with other morphs either rare or non-existent.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It has a more stark apparent whitish base colour apparent with a strong grey wash  on the head and mantle as well as strong herringbone patterning below. Altogether, it lacks the warmer tones common in more westerly tawny owls and its colouring is not dissimilar from a <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owl</a> but for the herringbone pattern.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grewal_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grewal-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blanford_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blanford-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Although at times apparently hypothesized as a separate form,<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> most authors continue to retain it as a proper subspecies of tawny owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Another distinct feature of <i>S. a. biddulphi</i> is its relatively large size and it appears to be the largest bodied race of tawny owl, although published weights are not known.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">Wing chord</a> in males was found to be 285 to 323&#160;mm (11.2 to 12.7&#160;in) whilst that of females measures 320 to 345&#160;mm (12.6 to 13.6&#160;in). The tail may measure 191 to 210&#160;mm (7.5 to 8.3&#160;in) while a single bird had a tarsal length of 51&#160;mm (2.0&#160;in) and two birds had bill lengths of 33 and 35&#160;mm (1.3 and 1.4&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i>S. a. willkonskii</i>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> to N <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> up to <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeastern Europe">southeastern Europe</a>
</td>
<td>(<a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Aleksandrovich_Menzbier" class="mw-redirect" title="Mikhail Aleksandrovich Menzbier">Menzbier</a>, 1896)
</td>
<td>In likelihood, this subspecies includes the formerly described race of <i>S. a. obscurata</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> On the whole, this race tends to be somewhat more richly coloured than the nominate subspecies. Particularly unique within this subspecies is a dark morph which is somewhat rufous but can grade into an almost <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> brown hue.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Although some authors consider this a small subspecies,<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> measurements suggest it is more so of intermediate size.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In fact, average sizes may be exceed those of nominate race tawny owls from further north in Europe and the male song may consequently have a slightly deeper tone as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Shekhovtsov_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shekhovtsov-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">Wing chord</a> in males may measure 255 to 296&#160;mm (10.0 to 11.7&#160;in) while females may measure 282 to 305&#160;mm (11.1 to 12.0&#160;in). Furthermore, weight of one male <i>S. a. willkonskii</i> was 510&#160;g (1.12&#160;lb) while one female weighed 582&#160;g (1.283&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i>S. a. sanctinicolai</i>
</td>
<td>W <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, NE <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>
</td>
<td>(Zarudny, 1905)<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</td>
<td>This little known subspecies is apparently a rather pale and washed-out form, as excepted for a species that lives in <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">subdesert region</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Although the only known measurements obtained have been of wing chord it appears to be one of the smaller forms of the tawny owl. Males may measure from 255 to 273&#160;mm (10.0 to 10.7&#160;in) and females have been known to measure 270 to 285&#160;mm (10.6 to 11.2&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i>S. a. harmsi</i>
</td>
<td>The area once known as <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a>, which today includes portions of six various countries.
</td>
<td>(Zarudny, 1911)<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</td>
<td>This is a relatively dark hued form, which may be in some way intermediate with the <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_owl" title="Himalayan owl">Himalayan owl</a> based on colouring but is still considered part of the tawny owl species.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> This race is quite large based on wing chord dimensions, and may rival <i>S. a. biddulphi</i> as the largest form of tawny owl. Measurements for males are 303 to 316&#160;mm (11.9 to 12.4&#160;in) while females they are 318 to 332&#160;mm (12.5 to 13.1&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i>S. a. siberiae</i>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Central_Russia" title="Central Russia">Central Russia</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Urals</a> to about the <a href="/wiki/Irtysh_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Irtysh river">Irtysh river</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Siberia" title="Western Siberia">Western Siberia</a>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Georgi_Petrovich_Dementiev" title="Georgi Petrovich Dementiev">Dementiev</a>, 1933
</td>
<td>This race is paler still than the nominate race with a large amount of dazzling white apparent on the sparsely marked underside, which tends to bare relatively few crossbars.<sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> This is a relatively large subspecies, being fairly similar in size to the nominate birds from <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This race is up to 12% larger than <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central European</a> nominate birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bergmann_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergmann-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">Wing chord</a> may measure from 280 to 300&#160;mm (11 to 12&#160;in) in males and from 301 to 311&#160;mm (11.9 to 12.2&#160;in) in females. A single owl measured 175&#160;mm (6.9&#160;in) in tail length and 33&#160;mm (1.3&#160;in) in bill length.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Unexpectedly, the reported weights for <i>S. a. siberiae</i> are not high relative to most reported in Europe and come in at a similar range as those reported for linearly rather smaller populations such as <i>S. a. sylvatica</i> in France.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baudvin_35-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baudvin-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Reported body mass for <i>S. a. siberiae</i> is 450 to 490&#160;g (0.99 to 1.08&#160;lb) in males and 590 to 680&#160;g (1.30 to 1.50&#160;lb) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Maghreb_owl" title="Maghreb owl">Maghreb owl</a> (<i>Strix mauritanica</i>) was recently split from this species.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>This species is found through much of <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, though spotty distribution here, with the largest gap where absent being in southeastern Spain (where still not completely absent).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl is also found throughout <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, but is not present in some of less well wooded areas of northern Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Their range is almost continuously from throughout <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> to <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">eastern Europe</a> within mainland Europe and continuously from <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> in the north down through most of Italy (including northern <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Heinzel_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heinzel-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls may be absent to rare in some swaths of southeastern Europe such as smallish parts of <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>, central <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> where the habitat probably becomes too mountainous and is similarly absent in the mountainous parts of <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> and northernmost <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, the tawny owl ranges through much of southern and central <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> (where they probably reach their northern limit as a species in central <a href="/wiki/Nordland" title="Nordland">Nordland</a>), southern <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> (up to <a href="/wiki/Dalarna" title="Dalarna">Dalarna</a> and southeastern <a href="/wiki/Norrland" title="Norrland">Norrland</a>) and southern <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> (jogging up slightly farther north along the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Their occurrence in Finland is quite recent, with the species estimated to have colonized the country independently around the year 1878, and, possibly in sync with <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">warming temperatures</a>, tawny owls have expanded their range in other relatively northern countries like Norway, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Merikallio_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merikallio-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl is considered a rare vagrant to the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Snow_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Also, the tawny owl ranges throughout coastal (to the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>) and central <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, far western <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, northernmost <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, northeastern <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, northern and southeastern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and western, northern and central <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Heinzel_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heinzel-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> After a wide gap of distribution, the range reassumes in central and eastern <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a> and northeasternmost <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (i.e. the area once consider <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a>). After another gap, the range resumes in northeasternmost <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, eastern Tajikistan and <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">north India</a> (mostly western <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir_(state)" title="Jammu and Kashmir (state)">Jammu and Kashmir</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl is also distributed in a large portion of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, though mainly the southwestern part, ranging up as far north up to about the city of <a href="/wiki/Petrozavodsk" title="Petrozavodsk">Petrozavodsk</a> in the west, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tolvayarvi" title="Lake Tolvayarvi">Lake Tolvayarvi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kama_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Kama River">Kama River</a> in the central part with the range stopping at roughly the <a href="/wiki/Irtysh_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Irtysh river">Irtysh river</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Siberia" title="Western Siberia">western Siberia</a> thence more or less continuously from there down into northwestern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Records of the species expanding their range along the Irtysh and far the west up into <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Karelia" title="Republic of Karelia">Karelia</a> may show that the species is expanding its range north much as it is in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Neufeldt_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeldt-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Epping_Forest_3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Epping_Forest_3.JPG/220px-Epping_Forest_3.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Epping_Forest_3.JPG/330px-Epping_Forest_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Epping_Forest_3.JPG/440px-Epping_Forest_3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4048" data-file-height="3040" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Epping_Forest_3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient deciduous woodland is a favoured habitat.</div></div></div>
<p>The preferred habitat of the tawny owl is <a href="/wiki/Temperate_deciduous_forest" title="Temperate deciduous forest">temperate deciduous forest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forest">mixed forest</a> with some access to <a href="/wiki/Woodland_edge" title="Woodland edge">clearings</a>. They too may habituate to <a href="/wiki/Riparian_zone" title="Riparian zone">riverine forests</a>, <a href="/wiki/Park" title="Park">parks</a>, large <a href="/wiki/Garden" title="Garden">gardens</a> with old trees, open landscapes with wooded patches and avenues of trees in open <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The species prefers "richly structured habitat" with <a href="/wiki/Old-growth_forest" title="Old-growth forest">old, mature trees</a> available.<sup id="cite_ref-Cesaris_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cesaris-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Since they naturally tend to utilize <a href="/wiki/Tree_hollow" title="Tree hollow">tree hollows</a> as nesting sites, sections of forest or woods with available <a href="/wiki/Snag_(ecology)" title="Snag (ecology)">snags</a> may be ideal.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> They tend to occupy pure <a href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">coniferous forest</a> only near edges or when <a href="/wiki/Clearing_(geography)" title="Clearing (geography)">clearings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glade_(geography)" title="Glade (geography)">glades</a> exist. Often areas in the conifer forest, especially the <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> in the north, where the tawnys will occur show a mixture of some deciduous tree growth such as <a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">birches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In the taiga-dominant environments of vast Russia, tawny owls are usually restricted to <a href="/wiki/Temperate_deciduous_forest" title="Temperate deciduous forest">broadleaf stands</a> often in river drainages, parks, orchards and cultivated lands, often where woods of <i><a href="/wiki/Quercus" class="mw-redirect" title="Quercus">Quercus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tilia" title="Tilia">Tilia</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Betula" class="mw-redirect" title="Betula">Betula</a></i> stand with plentiful broken snags and dead trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Locally, the tawny owl has been known to be adaptive to <a href="/wiki/Montane_ecosystems" title="Montane ecosystems">subalpine forest</a> dominated by conifers, such as the <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a> forests in the Spanish ranges of <a href="/wiki/Sierra_de_Gredos" title="Sierra de Gredos">Sierra de Gredos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sierra_de_Guadarrama" title="Sierra de Guadarrama">Sierra de Guadarrama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bernis_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernis-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in southern <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, they reported occur in <a href="/wiki/Spruce" title="Spruce">spruce</a>-<a href="/wiki/Fir" title="Fir">fir</a> dominated forests.<sup id="cite_ref-Kajtoch_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kajtoch-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Also the species can habituate to <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocky</a> areas as long as they have scattered trees and <a href="/wiki/Bush_(plant)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bush (plant)">bushes</a> from which to execute hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Locally, tawny owls are quite adaptive to living near or in human settlements, extending to <a href="/wiki/Town" title="Town">towns</a> or <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">cities</a>, most often within <a href="/wiki/Garden" title="Garden">timbered gardens</a> or tree-line pavement areas. They have adapted to living in parks or wooded suburban fringes of almost every major European city, including <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> and <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> They also live in and around even larger cities just outside of Europe, such as <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Arslangundogdu_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arslangundogdu-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sharikov_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharikov-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Although tawny owls occur in urban environments, they are less likely to occur at sites with high noise levels at night.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> While this owl can settle in very <a href="/wiki/Secondary_forest" title="Secondary forest">young forest</a> as long as <a href="/wiki/Nest_box" title="Nest box">nest boxes</a> are available, woods with trees too young to support typical hunting behaviours from a prominent perch may be suboptimal.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, it was found that nest boxes would booster the population in openings of the forest, interiors of mature forest and even <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a> but no increase was noted in young forest in a state of recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-Rumbutis_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rumbutis-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In the well-studied population of <a href="/wiki/Monks_Wood" title="Monks Wood">Monks Wood</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, those living in more continuous sections of the woods (stands exceeding 4&#160;ha (9.9 acres)) had more territorial skirmishes and overlapping territories while within farmland parts would be clustered around available wooded stands. In the Monks Woods, intermediate woods were probably preferable with less direct competition and more food was likely.<sup id="cite_ref-Redpath2_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redpath2-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sunde2_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde2-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In a <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romanian</a> study, tawny owls were rare in <a href="/w/index.php?title=Glade_(ecology)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Glade (ecology) (page does not exist)">glades</a> within the forest where substantial gaps occurred and were clustered around very old stands of trees, possibly being restricted from the more prey-rich glades by interspecific competition.<sup id="cite_ref-Bolboaca_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bolboaca-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> In central <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, 560 territories were studied in various habitats such as urban parks, <a href="/wiki/Mesophile" title="Mesophile">mesophilic woods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sclerophyll" title="Sclerophyll">sclerophyllous woods</a>, and mountainous <a href="/wiki/Beech" title="Beech">beech woods</a>, with the most attractive and highest density type being in sclerophyllous woods and lowest in urban woods and mountainous beech.<sup id="cite_ref-Salvati_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salvati-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Generally, tawny owls occur in <a href="/wiki/Lowland" class="mw-redirect" title="Lowland">lowland</a> areas but also may occur in mountainous areas (i.e. not exceeding 550&#160;m (1,800&#160;ft) in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Baxter_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baxter-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> They generally do not exceed 1,600 to 1,800&#160;m (5,200 to 5,900&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Metres_above_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Metres above sea level">above sea level</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> but may live at up to 2,650&#160;m (8,690&#160;ft) on <a href="/wiki/Piz_Lagrev" title="Piz Lagrev">Piz Lagrev</a> in <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Glutz_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glutz-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cucco_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cucco-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls may live at elevations of over 2,000&#160;m (6,600&#160;ft) in parts of <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tien_Shen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tien Shen (page does not exist)">Tien Shen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The species may even occur at elevations of up to 4,200&#160;m (13,800&#160;ft) in the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_in_the_Teufelsbruch_swamp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Strix_aluco_in_the_Teufelsbruch_swamp.jpg/220px-Strix_aluco_in_the_Teufelsbruch_swamp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Strix_aluco_in_the_Teufelsbruch_swamp.jpg/330px-Strix_aluco_in_the_Teufelsbruch_swamp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Strix_aluco_in_the_Teufelsbruch_swamp.jpg/440px-Strix_aluco_in_the_Teufelsbruch_swamp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_in_the_Teufelsbruch_swamp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny owl in the Teufelsbruch swamp.</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_owl_at_night_(42511916510).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Tawny_owl_at_night_%2842511916510%29.jpg/220px-Tawny_owl_at_night_%2842511916510%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Tawny_owl_at_night_%2842511916510%29.jpg/330px-Tawny_owl_at_night_%2842511916510%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Tawny_owl_at_night_%2842511916510%29.jpg/440px-Tawny_owl_at_night_%2842511916510%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3508" data-file-height="2338" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_owl_at_night_(42511916510).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tawny owls are quite <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnal</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny owl is generally quite <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnal</a>, but are sometimes briefly <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">active</a> during daylight. This is usually the case when young have to be fed and male owls may need to be active continuously for up to 11 hours in order to obtain enough prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In a probably exceptional event, some tawny owls were observed to mix with a flock of <a href="/wiki/Black-headed_gull" title="Black-headed gull">black-headed gulls</a> (<i>Chroicocephalus ridibundus</i>) in extracting <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a> on a plowed field in England in broad daylight.<sup id="cite_ref-Beven_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beven-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Of the three European owls in the <i>Strix</i> genus, the tawny is by far the least prone to be active during daylight.<sup id="cite_ref-Korpimaki_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korpimaki-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Nocturnal activity by tawny owls starts on average 18â€“22 minutes earlier and ends on average 10 minutes later than that of nearby <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wille_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wille-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Radio study in Monks Woods, revealed that upon nightfall, males nesting in continuous woodland spent 40% less time flying, covering an average distance of 74.9&#160;m (246&#160;ft) per hour, than those nesting in farmland, which covered an average of 148&#160;m (486&#160;ft) per hour. The males would perch for about 8 minutes on average.<sup id="cite_ref-Redpath3_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redpath3-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> These owls may roost by day amongst dense foliage, quite often on a branch close to the trunk, or in a natural hole in a tree or rock formation, in a hole or crevice of a wall.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> They at times will make use of manmade perches in suburban areas, such as <a href="/wiki/Utility_pole" title="Utility pole">utility poles</a>, peaked <a href="/wiki/Roof" title="Roof">roofs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chimney" title="Chimney">chimney</a> pots, tall <a href="/wiki/Fence" title="Fence">fences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billboard" title="Billboard">billboards</a> or <a href="/wiki/Television_antenna" title="Television antenna">television antenna</a> by dusk, while during the day they often tuck away in <a href="/wiki/Holly" title="Holly">hollies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">evergreens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oaks</a> and/or thick <a href="/wiki/Hedera" title="Hedera">ivy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> On occasion, they may found roosting even in the <a href="/wiki/Attic" title="Attic">attics</a> of large <a href="/wiki/Building" title="Building">buildings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barn" title="Barn">barns</a> or <a href="/wiki/Shed" title="Shed">sheds</a>, inside <a href="/wiki/Steeple" title="Steeple">church towers</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Chimney" title="Chimney">chimneys</a> of <a href="/wiki/House" title="House">houses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> One may be able to locate tawny owls by looking for <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">whitewash</a> but, unlike long-eared owls, tawny owls changes perch sites with some regularity so they tend to be less detectable overall.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Often finding tawny owls during daylight is done by listening for noisy <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_(animal_behavior)" title="Mobbing (animal behavior)">mobbing</a> of a discovered owl by other birds, especially by large and/or bold <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerines</a>, or by <a href="/wiki/Red_squirrel" title="Red squirrel">squirrels</a> during the day. Usually, the often fairly <a href="/wiki/Somnolence" title="Somnolence">drowsy</a> owls are unable to counterattack or kill their wary tormentors and may at times depart and try to seek out another roost.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_radio_telemetry" title="Wildlife radio telemetry">radiotelemetry</a> study of 22 owls in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> researched the effect perch use has in mitigating potential mobbings or predation acts. It was found that juveniles were more likely to use to secluded, hidden roosts whereas adults with hatched young through independent young were more likely to perch in the open apparently to protect their offspring. Adults were more likely to perch in open and closer to the ground when prey supplies were lower than were they were not.<sup id="cite_ref-Bolstad_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bolstad-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Though this is generally a quite cold-hardy species, a study near the northern limits of the species range in central <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> showed that due to <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermoregulation</a> that the owls locally had to compensate for the climate by conserving energy via incremental feeding activity.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territoriality_and_movements">Territoriality and movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Territoriality and movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waldkauz-Strix_aluco.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Waldkauz-Strix_aluco.jpg/220px-Waldkauz-Strix_aluco.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Waldkauz-Strix_aluco.jpg/330px-Waldkauz-Strix_aluco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Waldkauz-Strix_aluco.jpg/440px-Waldkauz-Strix_aluco.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waldkauz-Strix_aluco.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Brown individual, probably of subspecies <i>Strix aluco aluco</i>, in typical deciduous setting emerging from its tree hollow home.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny owl is a highly territorial owl that seldom leaves its home range. Tawny owls maintain territories through the signature male and female hooting songs.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Although they tend to most vigorously defend their territories in autumn, when year-old birds may try to supplant either member of a pair (though often unsuccessfully), and least so when actively incubating and brooding in spring and early summer, these owls can easily be provoked to defend their territory at any time of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Not infrequently, territorial fights become heated, potentially drawing all members of two pairs and/or escalating into a potentially fatal physical confrontations, and may be embellished with bill-snapping and wing raising.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Southern_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> During male territorial displays, after giving chase, the initial male is often chased right back, occasionally seesawing as such multiple times, occasionally hitting branches or wrestling one another to the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Hirons_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Occasional fights with long-eared owls along territorial edges are recorded too.<sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In Septemberâ€“December in Wytham Woods, 0.42 boundary disputes were recorded per hour in woods and 0.14 per hour at night in farmland, most occurred when pairs were within 3&#160;m (9.8&#160;ft) of each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Southern_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Territories tend to be markedly stable over time, in some pairs at <a href="/wiki/Wytham" title="Wytham">Wytham Woods</a>, territorial lines have been roughly the same over a 2 decade period.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Single pairs have been known maintained territories for up to 10 years in Russia and even up to 13 year in the Berlin area.<sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_110-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Gribskov" title="Gribskov">Gribskov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, the overlapping mutual range of both members of a pair averaged 82% in summer and 56% in winter, while on average 9% of the home range overlapped with neighboring pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde3_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde3-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Habitat appears to be key in territory size, i.e. in English farmland (<a href="/wiki/Warwickshire" title="Warwickshire">Warwickshire</a>) (10) territories were inversely related to the amount of closed woodland they contained, whereas in fragmented woodland (<a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire" title="Cambridgeshire">Cambridgeshire</a>) (23) territories were dependent strongly on the size of "core" woodland with owls in smaller or more isolated stands having larger territories.<sup id="cite_ref-Redpath2_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redpath2-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> In Wytham Woods, territories average around 7.3&#160;ha (18 acres) on sparse <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone ground</a>, but are 13.8&#160;ha (34 acres) on average where dense <a href="/wiki/Ground_cover" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground cover">ground cover</a> grows over <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay earth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Southern_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Subsequently, in British deciduous forests it was estimated that the average territory would be around 18.2&#160;ha (45 acres) in deciduous woods, 37.4&#160;ha (92 acres) in mixed farmland and 46.1&#160;ha (114 acres) in spruce stands.<sup id="cite_ref-Southern_44-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hirons2_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons2-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> In farmland areas of <a href="/wiki/Aberdeenshire" title="Aberdeenshire">Aberdeenshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, the defended border of the territory was up to 3&#160;km (1.9&#160;mi), with 17-40% of the territories were used exclusively for hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-Hardy2_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hardy2-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Spanish studies, in <a href="/wiki/Bizkaia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bizkaia">Bizkaia</a>, show a fairly low density of around 0.72 territories per square kilometer for 1704 occupied territories found in area of 2,348&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (907&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia2_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia2-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Lower still densities were found in <a href="/wiki/Murcia" title="Murcia">Murcia</a> far to the south in Spain, where the tawny owls must nest on rock formations, with 17 territories were found per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Sanchez-Zapata_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-Zapata-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>, in prime areas, territories are often about 25 to 35&#160;ha (62 to 86 acres), seldom to 50&#160;ha (120 acres), and have a defended boundary of about 2 to 3&#160;km (1.2 to 1.9&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Study blocks of western <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> held 42 territories on 50&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (19&#160;sq&#160;mi) and 21 territories in 25&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (9.7&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Ziesemer_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ziesemer-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere in central Europe, in <a href="/wiki/Koz%C5%82owiecki_forest" title="KozÅ‚owiecki forest">KozÅ‚owiecki forest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, pair occupancy increased from 2.4 to 4.6 per 10&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.9&#160;sq&#160;mi) between 1991 and 2006, due to conserving of forest, i.e. trees with cavities, and increasingly mild weather.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiacek_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiacek-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> The Polish city of <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> was found to hold 1.2-1.6 pairs per 10&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.9&#160;sq&#160;mi) in the city and 0.8-1 pairs per 10&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.9&#160;sq&#160;mi) in the general area, with 40-60 pairs found in Warsaw metropolitan.<sup id="cite_ref-Jablonski_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jablonski-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> In the Polish area of <a href="/wiki/Lubin" title="Lubin">Lubin</a>, territory sizes averaged 18.8&#160;ha (46 acres) with much variation based on pair density and season, with the smallest territories down to 10.8&#160;ha (27 acres) in summer and the largest in autumn at 30.9&#160;ha (76 acres).<sup id="cite_ref-Cios_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cios-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In the city centre of <a href="/wiki/Pavia" title="Pavia">Pavia</a> in Italy, the average number of pairs per square kilometer was 0.9-1.1 or an average territory size of 17.9&#160;ha (44 acres), while in the rural areas nearby the average territory size was 22&#160;ha (54 acres). Higher densities still were detected in the nearby <a href="/wiki/Po_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Po valley">Po plains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti6_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti6-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti7_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti7-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a study of 586 territories on 22 study plots in central Italy, average territory size in peak thermophilous woods was 7.1&#160;ha (18 acres) while other woodland types from urban parks to montane beech habitats ranged in average size from 10.8 to 22.4&#160;ha (27 to 55 acres).<sup id="cite_ref-Salvati2_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salvati2-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ranazzi_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ranazzi-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Territory size varied in the area of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> by habitat, with wooded city parks, with 3.3 territories per square kilometer, and well-wooded suburbs, with 5.7 territories per square kilometer, holding peak numbers among the habitat types and having an average territory size of 17.6&#160;ha (43 acres), while developed areas of the city, rural areas and farmland (where average territory was 183.4&#160;ha (453 acres)) all held considerably lower densities of territories.<sup id="cite_ref-Ranazzi2_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ranazzi2-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ranazzi3_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ranazzi3-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> A few recorded territories in Denmark were found to be 27 to 50&#160;ha (67 to 124 acres) in size.<sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  Two studies in <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> placed territory size at 65 to 75&#160;ha (160 to 190 acres), elsewhere it was estimated in Belgium that there was 1 pair per 72&#160;ha (180 acres).<sup id="cite_ref-Delmee_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delmee-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dambiermont_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dambiermont-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>'s montane <a href="/wiki/Bjelasica" title="Bjelasica">Bjelasica</a> area, 6.1 territories were registered per 10&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.9&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>, the average density of territories was 8.3 per 10&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.9&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Bolboaca2_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bolboaca2-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> In Sweden, larger territories are necessary, i.e. 2 mature females over 89 and 146&#160;ha (220 and 360 acres).<sup id="cite_ref-Nilsson_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nilsson-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> A similarly very large range was reported for tawny owls in the <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a> area of <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde4_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde4-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The tawny owl is a not a migratory bird and adults tend to be highly residential, maintaining their home range and territory throughout the year. However, juvenile dispersal can occur over dozens to rarely hundreds of kilometres.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In southern Finland, juveniles rarely move more than 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) away from their nest of origin, doing so in <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">multidirectional movements</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In Sweden, most movements by juveniles are to less than 50&#160;km (31&#160;mi) away from their nest of origin, rarely more so. Exceptionally a juvenile tawny was recorded to cover 745&#160;km (463&#160;mi) northwesterly from <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4sterg%C3%B6tland" title="VÃ¤stergÃ¶tland">VÃ¤stergÃ¶tland</a> to <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4sterbotten" title="VÃ¤sterbotten">VÃ¤sterbotten</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Olsson_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The record movement recorded for a tawny owl from Scandinavia (or possibly from anywhere) apparently is 745&#160;km (463&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Further south in Europe, long-distance movements tend to be much rarer, and perhaps such movements are rarer than any other owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glutz_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glutz-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Rare records show movements of up to 270 to 450&#160;km (170 to 280&#160;mi) movements from the northern reaches of <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glutz_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glutz-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_biology">Dietary biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Dietary biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%C5%A0umska_sova_(Strix_aluco),_kand%C5%BEe;_Tawny_Owl_claws.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/%C5%A0umska_sova_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_kand%C5%BEe%3B_Tawny_Owl_claws.jpg/220px-%C5%A0umska_sova_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_kand%C5%BEe%3B_Tawny_Owl_claws.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/%C5%A0umska_sova_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_kand%C5%BEe%3B_Tawny_Owl_claws.jpg/330px-%C5%A0umska_sova_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_kand%C5%BEe%3B_Tawny_Owl_claws.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/%C5%A0umska_sova_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_kand%C5%BEe%3B_Tawny_Owl_claws.jpg/440px-%C5%A0umska_sova_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_kand%C5%BEe%3B_Tawny_Owl_claws.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3170" data-file-height="2415" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%C5%A0umska_sova_(Strix_aluco),_kand%C5%BEe;_Tawny_Owl_claws.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The fairly powerful talons of a tawny owl.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny owl is an opportunistic and generalized predator. Peak hunting activity tends to occur largely between <a href="/wiki/Dusk" title="Dusk">dusk</a> to <a href="/wiki/Midnight" title="Midnight">midnight</a>, with owls often following an erratic hunting pattern, perhaps to sites where previous hunts were successful.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Redpath3_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redpath3-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> When feeding young, hunting may need to be prolonged into daylight in the early morning.<sup id="cite_ref-Hirons_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hardy3_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hardy3-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Based on hand-reared young owls that re-released into the wild, hunting behaviour is quite <a href="/wiki/Innatism" title="Innatism">innate</a> rather than learned.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Normally this owl hunts from a perch.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Perching bouts usually last from about 8 to 14 minutes depending largely on habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-Redpath3_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redpath3-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owl's hunting from a perch or pole can recall a <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">buzzard</a> and the two take similar prey sizes as well. However, high initial speed and maneuvering among trees and bushes with great dexterity may allow it to surprise relatively large prey, more like a <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">goshawk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl is capable of lifting and carrying off in flight individual prey weighing up to at least 320&#160;g (11&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Bernhoft-Osa_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernhoft-Osa-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Their middle talon, the most enlarged claw on owls, measures an average of 19.1&#160;mm (0.75&#160;in). While not as large as those of the <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owl</a>, the talons are extremely sharp, stout and quite decurved. The claws are considered to be visibly more overdeveloped than those of other European mid-sized owls and the footspan including the claws is fairly larger as well, at an average of about 13.4&#160;cm (5.3&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_31-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The hunting owl often extends its wings to balance and control prey upon impact.<sup id="cite_ref-Meinerzhagen_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meinerzhagen-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Alternatively, this species may hunt from flight. This occurs from 2 to 3&#160;m (6.6 to 9.8&#160;ft) over the ground, often over open habitats such as <a href="/wiki/Shrub" title="Shrub">bushes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marsh</a> or <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a>, forming a quartering or zigzag pattern over the opening. During these flights they cover about 30 to 50&#160;m (98 to 164&#160;ft) before changing direction.<sup id="cite_ref-Nilsson2_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nilsson2-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Hunting from flight was surprisingly prevalent in a Swedish study of two radio-tagged birds, with 34% of study time spent hunting from flight while 40% of the study time was spent on hunting from a perch.<sup id="cite_ref-Nilsson2_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nilsson2-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> In a similar study in England, less than 1% of time was spent hunting from flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Redpath3_137-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redpath3-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> In a more deliberate variation of hunting from flight, the hunting owl may examine crags and nest boxes or also <a href="/wiki/Levitation" title="Levitation">hover</a> around prey roosts. In the latter type of hunts, the tawny owls may strike branches and/or beat their wings together in front of denser foliage, bushes or conifers in order to disturb and flush prey such as small <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a>, or may dive directly into said foliage.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meinerzhagen_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meinerzhagen-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Hovering has also been recorded in differing circumstances, including one incidence of an owl hunting a <a href="/wiki/Bank_swallow" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank swallow">small bird</a> that was caught on the wing after a hovering flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Ruthke_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruthke-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls have also taken bats on the wing as well (such as ones snatched from near <a href="/wiki/Street_light" title="Street light">streep lamps</a> when attempting to hunt themselves) and have been seen to <a href="/wiki/Hawking_(birds)" title="Hawking (birds)">hawk</a> large, relatively slow-flying <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> such as some <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moths</a> in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar" title="Caterpillar">Caterpillars</a> may too be taken from trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_168-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Usually these hunting variations are correlated with poor weather hampering the capture of preferred prey.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls eat <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">worms</a> with relative frequency, as they often hear them apparently from below the surface and snatch them up from shallow dirt or below leaf litter. Their worm-hunting style recalls worm hunting techniques by most other birds and they were recorded to eat 0.39 worms per minute during an hour of observation in England and were sometimes seen to feed on worms during daylight.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beven_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beven-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Macdonald_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macdonald-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Other hunting from the ground has been observed, often of <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, but tawny owls have also been reported to "leap" upon from a ground vantage point in order to capture a <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">vole</a>, quite like <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">foxes</a> often do.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schnurre_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnurre-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> There are now many accounts of tawny owls feeding on <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a> from a wide range of sources, including <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_rat" title="Black rat">rats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trout" title="Trout">trout</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Speiballen_eines_Waldkauzes(Strix_aluco(L.)).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Speiballen_eines_Waldkauzes%28Strix_aluco%28L.%29%29.jpg/220px-Speiballen_eines_Waldkauzes%28Strix_aluco%28L.%29%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Speiballen_eines_Waldkauzes%28Strix_aluco%28L.%29%29.jpg/330px-Speiballen_eines_Waldkauzes%28Strix_aluco%28L.%29%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Speiballen_eines_Waldkauzes%28Strix_aluco%28L.%29%29.jpg/440px-Speiballen_eines_Waldkauzes%28Strix_aluco%28L.%29%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2205" data-file-height="1839" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Speiballen_eines_Waldkauzes(Strix_aluco(L.)).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A partially dissected pellet of a tawny owl.</div></div></div>
<p>Upon capture, small prey like <a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">shrews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> are often swallowed whole, while others may be torn into pieces. Often prey is dismembered in order to more easily ingest it whole, i.e. decapitating <a href="/wiki/Mouse" title="Mouse">mice</a>, removing the legs from <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a> while birds like <a href="/wiki/Old_World_sparrow" title="Old World sparrow">sparrows</a> are also regularly decapitated (with the head often eaten separately) and nearly all avian prey is plucked before being consumed.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thiollay_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thiollay-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Butikofer_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butikofer-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> One tawny owl was observed to eat a <a href="/wiki/Red_squirrel" title="Red squirrel">squirrel</a> by leaving the head intact and peeling the skin back from the neck, apparently leaving bones in place while consuming the flesh.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Indigestible items, including fur, feathers, bones (which sometimes visibly protrude out of the peller), sometimes <a href="/wiki/Intestine" class="mw-redirect" title="Intestine">intestines</a> and invertebrate <a href="/wiki/Carapace" title="Carapace">carapaces</a>, are regurgitated in large <a href="/wiki/Pellet_(ornithology)" title="Pellet (ornithology)">pellets</a>, that can be anywhere in typical size from 20.3 to 67&#160;mm (0.80 to 2.64&#160;in) long with a diameter of 17 to 30&#160;mm (0.67 to 1.18&#160;in). The pellets are typically grey coloured and are found in groups under trees used for roosting or nesting. At least some tawny owl pellets can measure up to 84&#160;mm (3.3&#160;in) long and can include large objects such as an intact 10&#160;cm (3.9&#160;in) bill of a <a href="/wiki/Common_snipe" title="Common snipe">snipe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thiollay_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thiollay-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown2_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Undigested material coughed up often reveals different prey than pellets.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Estimated daily food requirements for a tawny owl is 73.5&#160;g (2.59&#160;oz), which is proportionately lower (at about 14% of their own body mass) than the estimates for other medium-sized owls in Europe (at 23-26% of their own body mass), therefore tawny owls can appear to live off of relatively little food quite efficiently.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prey_spectrum">Prey spectrum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Prey spectrum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The tawny owl takes an extremely wide range of prey species. The global prey spectrum for tawny owls includes well over 400 prey species.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> They generally prefer small <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> in their diet, especially various species of <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodent</a>, where they are available.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> However, they are one are the <a href="/wiki/Generalist_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Generalist species">least specialized</a> owls in Europe when it comes to prey selection and can broadly be described as extremely opportunistic.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls respond to access of prey concentrations of virtually any variety, including <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> as well as sometimes <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, by taking them in large numbers, sometimes equal or even (more infrequently) greater numbers than mammalian prey.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Capizzi_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capizzi-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> The difference between the generalist tawny owl and a specialized <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodent</a>-hunter like the <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> was illustrated in a semi-captive experience where the two owl species were exposed to different <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">classes</a> of wild prey as they encountered it. In this experiment, only small mammals and roosting <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">sparrows</a> were attacked and eaten by both, though flying sparrrows were avoided by long-eared owls and not by the tawnys. In the stated study, the tawny owls would kill and eat amphibians and fish, while the long-eared owls would rarely kill and never eat these types of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Raber_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raber-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In a study of five European <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biomes</a>, with about 45 prey species per biome, the tawny owl was estimated to have tied for the second most prey species per biome after the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Another European study found the mean <a href="/wiki/Niche_differentiation" title="Niche differentiation">food niche breadth</a>,  i.e. the estimated average by number of prey species per nest or study site, the tawny owl surpassed all European owls within the two of the three main regions of non-British Europe, with 5.84 mean food niche breadth in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a> and 4.3 food niche breadth in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> region. In the latter study, the eagle-owl food niche breadth was listed as 2.4 and 3.3 in these regions, respectively (tawny owls were excluded from analysis in the Scandivanian region due to their marginal range there).<sup id="cite_ref-Herrera_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herrera-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl mostly focuses on fairly small-sized prey. One estimation of the mean prey size taken in all of Europe for the tawny owl was 34.5&#160;g (1.22&#160;oz). In northern and central Europe, older studies place the mean prey size taken as usually between 29 and 40&#160;g (1.0 and 1.4&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_168-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Another study, of the aforementioned 5 European biomes, showed a drastically lower mean estimated prey size of 18.6&#160;g (0.66&#160;oz), even slightly lower than the mean prey size taken by an owl like the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_pygmy_owl" title="Eurasian pygmy owl">Eurasian pygmy owl</a> (<i>Glaucidium passerinum</i>), which weighs about one-eighth as much as tawny owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Individual dietary studies show that the mean prey mass taken by tawny owls can vary from 12.6 to 130&#160;g (0.44 to 4.59&#160;oz) depending on prey access.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti_190-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Koning2_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koning2-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> A central Italian study showed how habitat type and resulting prey composition can vary mean prey size considerably, with <a href="/wiki/Montane_ecosystems" title="Montane ecosystems">broadleaf highland forest</a> having a mean prey mass of 26.6&#160;g (0.94&#160;oz), <a href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forest">mixed forest</a> having a mean mass of 37.7&#160;g (1.33&#160;oz), <a href="/wiki/Peri-urbanisation" title="Peri-urbanisation">urban areas</a> having a mean prey mass of 40.3&#160;g (1.42&#160;oz) and <a href="/wiki/Coppicing" title="Coppicing">coppice woodland</a> having a high mean prey mass of 73.1&#160;g (2.58&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Marchesi_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marchesi-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
