<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:R%C3%B6telmaus_I.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/R%C3%B6telmaus_I.jpg/220px-R%C3%B6telmaus_I.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/R%C3%B6telmaus_I.jpg/330px-R%C3%B6telmaus_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/R%C3%B6telmaus_I.jpg/440px-R%C3%B6telmaus_I.jpg 2x" data-file-width="665" data-file-height="410" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:R%C3%B6telmaus_I.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Bank_vole" title="Bank vole">bank vole</a> is a common prey.</div></div></div>
<p>Tawny owls will potentially take any small mammals that they encounter. This was illustrated in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> where the number of species taken by the owls was greater than the number of species <a href="/wiki/Biologist" title="Biologist">biologists</a> could capture themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> They primarily take and derive most of their <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a> from <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodent</a> prey.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Dietary staples in much of their range are in particular the long-tailed <i><a href="/wiki/Apodemus" title="Apodemus">Apodemus</a></i>, commonly called field or wood mice, and the short-tailed rodents known as <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>. Amongst voles, the widely distributed <a href="/wiki/Bank_vole" title="Bank vole">bank vole</a> (<i>Myodes glareolus</i>) tends to be the most favored type over large portions of the range, though different species of the <i><a href="/wiki/Microtus" title="Microtus">Microtus</a></i> genus can become locally rather prominent.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> <i>Microtus</i> voles tend to forage in more open habitats such as fields than do the wooded edge-favoring bank vole and especially <i>Apodemus</i> mice, and so are usually selected more so where the preferred rodent types are rare or absent.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Previous studies claimed that <i>Apodemus</i> mice were preferred where available over bank voles as the latter was considered "somewhat diurnal",<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> however bank voles like many voles are more correctly considered <a href="/wiki/Cathemerality" title="Cathemerality">cathemeral</a>, potentially active any time of day or night, and may actually be readily accessible to hunting tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Yatsiuk_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yatsiuk-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> However, the bank voles favoring of heavier <a href="/wiki/Ground_cover" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground cover">ground cover</a> does limit access to them in the warmer months, whereas <i>Apodemus</i> mice are more likely to continue forage on open ground adjacent to woods and tend to be preferred at this time.<sup id="cite_ref-Southern2_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern2-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> It was found that bank voles become more vulnerable to tawny owls in areas where enlarged <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a> herds consume more of the ground cover.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> A broadly study of different nations within <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a> found that <i>Apodemus</i> mice and bank voles could alternately take the primary food mantle, and that the variation of which was favorite was likely due to differing habitat and forest characteristics in the given regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Zmihorski_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zmihorski-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, bank and <i>Microtus</i> voles were taken more or less in equivalent occurrence to their observed populations in the field.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in Poland, they took <a href="/wiki/Yellow-necked_mouse" title="Yellow-necked mouse">yellow-necked mice</a> (<i>Apodemus flavicollis</i>) adults roughly in proportion to their occurrence in the wild. On the other hand, per the Polish study, juvenile yellow-necks were taken much less and subadults much more than their occurrence in the wild. Here, the tawny owls took slightly larger specimens on average than the average recorded in wild, at an estimated mean size taken of 34&#160;g (1.2&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> In a study from <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, yellow-necked mice and bank voles that were caught by tawny owls were disproportionately large, adult males (55% and 73% of the time, respectively).<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> In central <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, tawny and long-eared owls took <a href="/wiki/Common_vole" title="Common vole">common voles</a> (<i>Microtus arvalis</i>) than were 24% heavier on average than those encountered in the wild, which averaged 16.45&#160;g (0.580&#160;oz) (thus including younger voles). More surprisingly, the long-eared owls were taking voles averaging some 9% larger than those taken by tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Balciauskas_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balciauskas-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Wild mice, bank voles and, to a more pronounced extent, <i>Microtus</i> voles undergo <a href="/wiki/Population_cycle" title="Population cycle">population cycles</a> over a three-year (or sometimes four-year) span, which frequently requires the owls to alternate their foods when populations decline.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> This effect was studied in the British <a href="/wiki/Kielder_Forest" title="Kielder Forest">Kielder Forest</a> and the nearby <a href="/wiki/Kershope_Burn" title="Kershope Burn">Kershope Burn</a>. Here tawny owls are exceptionally dependent on <a href="/wiki/Field_vole" title="Field vole">field voles</a> (<i>Microtus agretis</i>) as food, constituting about 64.3% of 1220 prey items in the area, but the Kielder forest field vole population had an exceptional four-year drought whereas in the same time frame Kershope kept a more stable owl population seemingly because it retained the typical three-year cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-Petty3_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty3-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty4_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty4-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Wytham" title="Wytham">Wytham</a>, Britain, tawnys were thought to remove up to one-third of the local population of bank voles and one-third to three-quarters of the less numerous <a href="/wiki/Wood_mouse" title="Wood mouse">wood mouse</a> (<i>Apodemus sylvatica</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Southern2_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern2-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> On a 148&#160;ha (370 acres) plot of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Poland_Voivodeship" title="Greater Poland Voivodeship">Wielkopolska</a>, tawny owls are thought to remove an estimated 2,213 rodents annually, or 15 rodents per ha each year, which was about the same rate of loss of <a href="/wiki/Striped_field_mouse" title="Striped field mouse">striped field mouse</a> (<i>Apodemus agrarius</i>) per ha in the Warsaw area as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Goszczynski_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goszczynski-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bia%C5%82owie%C5%BCa_Forest" title="BiaÅ‚owieÅ¼a Forest">BiaÅ‚owieÅ¼a Forest</a>, tawny owls were estimated to remove in autumn 54% of the yellow-necked mice and 40% of bank voles.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the largest known European diet studies, rodents usually are predominant. Amongst 68,070 prey items in <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, the main prey were the yellow-necked mouse (23.8%), the bank vole (9.9%) and the <a href="/wiki/Common_vole" title="Common vole">common vole</a> (9.14%).<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, the same three main prey species led the foods amongst 17,433 prey items, with the yellow-necked at 33.4%, the common vole at 15.7% and the bank vole at 11.2%.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Among prey groups in <a href="/wiki/Grunewald" title="Grunewald">Grunewald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, with 13,359 <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> prey items studied, <i>Apodemus</i> species made up 25.7% of the foods and <i>Microtus</i> voles of about four species made up a further 16.7%.<sup id="cite_ref-Wendland2_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland2-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> The diet differed in the German area of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herrnut&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Herrnut (page does not exist)">Herrnut</a>, where the common vole was dominant in the foods at 53.3% of 8513 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In a little over half of about 15 smaller prey studies for tawny owls in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, mammals led the food composition of owls by number, but in different areas and habitats of the nation, yellow-necked mice, common voles and bank voles could be at the top of the list.<sup id="cite_ref-Gryz2_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gryz2-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goszczynski_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goszczynski-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goszczynski2_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goszczynski2-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Of 43,000 mammal prey items in an older large study of central Europe, 66% were bank or <i>Microtus</i> voles, while a further 24% were <i>Apodemus</i> species.<sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer2_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer2-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>  In <a href="/wiki/Bourgogne-Franche-Comt%C3%A9" title="Bourgogne-Franche-ComtÃ©">Bourgogne-Franche-ComtÃ©</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, amongst 51,743 prey items, <i>Apodemus</i> species, presumably dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Wood_mouse" title="Wood mouse">wood mouse</a>, made up 51.1% by number and 48.8% of the <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> followed by bank vole, at 20.4% by number and 15.6% by biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Baudvin2_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baudvin2-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> In western <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, the diet was similar but far more homogeneous, with <i>Apodemus</i> species at 74.3% and bank vole at 18.7% among 10,176 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Roulin_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roulin-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The northernmost food study for tawny owls thus far conducted showed that in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, field voles were the main food amongst 578 prey items, at 30.5%, with bank voles being supplemental at 8.7%. However, the second most commonly taken prey in Sweden is the much larger <a href="/wiki/European_water_vole" title="European water vole">European water vole</a> (<i>Arvicola amphibius</i>), which weighs an estimated mean of 177&#160;g (6.2&#160;oz), and presumably a very nutritious prey resource to these owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Lundberg_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lundberg-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> The easternmost food study thus far known was a small one of 201 prey items for the tawny owls in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, wherein the common vole was dominant at 72.6%.<sup id="cite_ref-Sharikov2_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharikov2-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Of similar <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, amongst 1236 prey items, the main foods were <a href="/wiki/Ural_field_mouse" title="Ural field mouse">Ural field mouse</a> (<i>Apodemus uralensis</i>) at 48.1% of the prey composition and <a href="/wiki/Edible_dormouse" title="Edible dormouse">edible dormouse</a> (<i>Glis glis</i>) at 15%.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl takes many species of <a href="/wiki/Dormouse" title="Dormouse">dormouse</a>, which are nocturnal, largely <a href="/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion" title="Arboreal locomotion">arboreal</a> and generally rarer within the forests and edges than common mice and vole prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Scaravelli_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scaravelli-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch2_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch2-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> While many dormice are smallish (roughly vole or mouse sized), the edible species is often more than five times larger, being close in dimensions to the European water vole. Therefore, the prey biomass must have been hearty in <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>, where the edible dormouse was the main food, at 24.1% of 529 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Other more easterly parts of Europe show relatively high balances of edible dormice as well, such as in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kuhar_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhar-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Another widely taken species is the <a href="/wiki/Hazel_dormouse" title="Hazel dormouse">hazel dormouse</a> (<i>Muscardinus avellanarius</i>), as well as at least three further species.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch2_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch2-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Another rodent of special interest due to its natural scarcity and its place in the diet of tawny owls is the <a href="/wiki/Northern_birch_mouse" title="Northern birch mouse">northern birch mouse</a> (<i>Sicista betulina</i>), which was found to constitute as much as 7% of the foods in some districts of <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, but only contributed 0.6% of the foods overall in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zmihorski2_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zmihorski2-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>All told, no less than 80 rodent species are known to be taken by tawny owls. While most of these are characteristic prey such as various voles and <a href="/wiki/Lemming" title="Lemming">lemmings</a> and any type of <a href="/wiki/Muridae" title="Muridae">murid rodent</a> from the smallest available <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_harvest_mouse" title="Eurasian harvest mouse">mice</a> to the largest available <a href="/wiki/Brown_rat" title="Brown rat">rats</a>, other rodents also taken.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Black_rat" title="Black rat">Black rats</a> (<i>Rattus rattus</i>) were noted to be the main prey for tawny owls in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, where they accounted for 35.3% by number of 351 prey items and 60.2% of the biomass, resulting in a relative high mean prey mass of 79.7&#160;g (2.81&#160;oz) here.<sup id="cite_ref-Sara_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sara-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Strong biomass contributions were noted of <a href="/wiki/Brown_rat" title="Brown rat">brown rats</a> (<i>Rattus norvegicus</i>) elsewhere such as in <a href="/wiki/Lublin" title="Lublin">Lublin</a> in Poland (wherein they accounted for 41.5% of the biomass) and in Algeria (wherein they accounted for about 20% of the biomass), although many rats taken are on the young side rather than large adults, especially of the large brown species.<sup id="cite_ref-Capizzi_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capizzi-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tergou_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tergou-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wiacek2_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiacek2-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl's prey spectrum also extends to less accessible prey like <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrels</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a>), with more or less all the species of Europe and western Asia known to be taken by these owls despite their <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnality</a>, as well as the nocturnal but scarce <a href="/wiki/Siberian_flying_squirrel" title="Siberian flying squirrel">flying squirrels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_110-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goszczynski_208-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goszczynski-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hirons3_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons3-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> The widespread <a href="/wiki/Red_squirrel" title="Red squirrel">red squirrel</a> (<i>Sciurus vulgaris</i>), estimated to weigh an average of 150 to 300&#160;g (5.3 to 10.6&#160;oz) when taken, appear to recognize the tawny owl as a serious threat, with ones exposed to recordings of their calls recorded to interrupt feedings, engage in rapid movements and scold harshly.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde4_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde4-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Capizzi_188-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capizzi-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty3_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty3-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hamster" title="Hamster">Hamsters</a> may too be taken despite favoring and occurring in more open habitats than those usually hunted by tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Kitowski_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitowski-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nedyalkov_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nedyalkov-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> In the southerly parts of the range, as they've acclimated to <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-desert</a>, tawny owls can sometimes partially off of quite different murid rodents like <a href="/wiki/Jird" class="mw-redirect" title="Jird">jirds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerbil" title="Gerbil">gerbils</a> as well as the non-murid <a href="/wiki/Spalax" title="Spalax">blind mole rats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nedyalkov_237-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nedyalkov-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch3_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch3-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Comay_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comay-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Rodent prey may range up to the size of probable juveniles of the non-native <a href="/wiki/Coypu" title="Coypu">nutria</a> (Myocastor coypus).<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Loirs_mangeant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Loirs_mangeant.jpg/220px-Loirs_mangeant.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Loirs_mangeant.jpg/330px-Loirs_mangeant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Loirs_mangeant.jpg/440px-Loirs_mangeant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1356" data-file-height="1351" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Loirs_mangeant.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Large prey for tawny owls can extend commonly in some areas to <a href="/wiki/Edible_dormouse" title="Edible dormouse">edible dormice</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">Shrews</a> are a common component of the foods of tawny owls, less so their larger but generally less numerous distant cousins such as <a href="/wiki/Mole_(animal)" title="Mole (animal)">moles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog" title="Hedgehog">hedgehogs</a>. More than 20 species of shrew are known in the foods of this owl.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> While usually secondary, shrews are widely present in the pellets and prey remains in most studies. Unlike some owls such as long-eared owls they do not seem to disdain these <a href="/wiki/Musk" title="Musk">musky</a>-tasting and slight insectivores.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Certainly the most reported variety would be the widespread <a href="/wiki/Common_shrew" title="Common shrew">common shrew</a> (<i>Sorex araneus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Exceptionally, in a large food study for <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, common shrews were the leading prey species, at 18.2% of 15,450 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In a much smaller study in Norway during the summer, the common shrew was the leading prey species, constituting 30.4% of 69 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde4_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde4-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> However, given their small size, with the common shrew being one of the larger available species at merely 8 to 11&#160;g (0.28 to 0.39&#160;oz), shrews are a marginal contributor to the owl's prey biomass and taken for subsistence until a more substantial food source is available.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Capizzi_188-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capizzi-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty5_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty5-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Exceptional quantities of shrews may be predicted in French studies (usually during preferred prey shortages), with shrew prey contributing up to 15% of the biomass overall and more locally, in the <a href="/wiki/Oignies" title="Oignies">Oignies</a>, to 29% of the biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Guerin_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guerin-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Despite the low numbers of moles that are usually hunted, species such as the <a href="/wiki/European_mole" title="European mole">European mole</a> (<i>Talpa europaea</i>) can be contribute heartily to the prey biomass, such as in <a href="/wiki/Wytham" title="Wytham">Wytham</a>, where the species made up 15.6% of the biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Although such prey is known to be relatively limited in the species' foods, tawny owls are known to hunt the smallest living mammal species (by weight), the 1.8&#160;g (0.063&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_shrew" title="Etruscan shrew">Etruscan shrew</a> (<i>Suncus etruscus</i>), up to the size of the largest mole, the 440&#160;g (16&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Russian_desman" title="Russian desman">Russian desman</a> (<i>Desmana moschata</i>), as well as perhaps larger still, some small adults of the <a href="/wiki/European_hedgehog" title="European hedgehog">European hedgehog</a> (<i>Erinaceus europaeus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Capizzi_188-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capizzi-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On occasion, tawny owls will prey on young <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">European rabbits</a> (<i>Oryctolagus cuniculus</i>) as well as very young <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a>. Mostly neonatal or scarcely older rabbits are taken, with a few studies estimating the mean weight as caught as only 100 to 350&#160;g (3.5 to 12.3&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Adanez_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adanez-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Capizzi_188-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capizzi-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty5_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty5-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Access to European rabbit was said to cause the mean prey mass of tawny owls in parts of the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> to an unprecidently high 130&#160;g (4.6&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Koning2_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koning2-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> One Spanish study claimed that up to 23% of the vertebrate prey for the tawny owl was made up of by rabbits, making them the smallest known avian predator to show a dependence on them.<sup id="cite_ref-Jaksic_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jaksic-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Though generally a minor part of the diet, a wide diversity of <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a> are taken by tawny owls, with over 30 species in their prey spectrum.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kowalski_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kowalski-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lesinski_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lesinski-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sharikov3_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharikov3-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> Usually less than 1% of vertebrate prey consists of bats but in Poland, dietary relations have been studied between tawny owls living near <a href="/wiki/Cave" title="Cave">bat caves</a> and urban bat roosts, and locally up to as much as 2% of the diet (and 5.3% of the mammalian foods) can consist of bats. Studies have indicated that bat species are more or less hunted in proportion to their occurrence in mixed colonies and are taken more so within urbanized environments as well as when staple rodent prey is low.<sup id="cite_ref-Kowalski_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kowalski-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lesinski_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lesinski-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kowalski2_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kowalski2-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, 252 attacks by tawny owls were recorded at a colony of <a href="/wiki/Geoffroy%27s_bat" title="Geoffroy&#39;s bat">Geoffroy's bats</a> (<i>Myotis emarginatus</i>), 31 of which were successful.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, it is estimated that tawny owls eliminate at minimum 140,000 individual bats annually.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> While most bats encountered (and hunted) are fairly small-bodied, tawny owls may hunt bats of all sizes available, from the roughly 4&#160;g (0.14&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_pipistrelle" title="Common pipistrelle">common pipistrelle</a> (<i>Pipistrellus pipistrellus</i>) to the 59&#160;g (2.1&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Greater_noctule" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater noctule">greater noctule</a> (<i>Nyctalus lasiopterus</i>) in Europe and to the 140&#160;g (4.9&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_fruit_bat" title="Egyptian fruit bat">Egyptian fruit bat</a> (<i>Rousettus aegyptiacus</i>) outside of Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch3_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch3-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lesinski_250-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lesinski-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> Other mammalian prey recorded have been <a href="/wiki/Mustelid" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustelid">mustelids</a>. The tawny owl is known to hunt both common <a href="/wiki/Weasel" title="Weasel">weasels</a> in Europe, including fairly large <a href="/wiki/Stoat" title="Stoat">stoats</a> (<i>Mustela erminea</i>), weighing averages of up to 200 to 300&#160;g (7.1 to 10.6&#160;oz), despite the potential risk of counterattacking by these bold and powerful hunters.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sunde4_161-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde4-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty5_242-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty5-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Traces of an even bigger mustelid have been found, the <a href="/wiki/European_pine_marten" title="European pine marten">European pine marten</a> (<i>Martes martes</i>), in the foods of tawny owls, though it is a considerable possibility that this was scavenged rather than killed by the owl, much like the verified case of tawny owls scavenging remains of <a href="/wiki/European_polecat" title="European polecat">European polecats</a> (<i>Mustela putorius</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tawny owls do not take birds as commonly as mammals. Unlike the unrelated lineages of <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal</a> <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>, owls in general seldom prefer avian prey, with most varieties preferring small mammals and/or insects, except on a local basis (the closest to a specialized hunter of other birds are some in the <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_owl" title="Pygmy owl">pygmy owl</a> genus).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls do opportunistically hunt birds through most of the range, however.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> When it comes to avian prey, there is little evidence that any particular kind is sought out and the owls are likely to randomly come across other birds as an alternate food choice.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Usually, European studies show that birds normally constitute less than 15% of the total foods of this owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer2_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer2-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> In central Europe within an older study, 6000 bird prey (or a little less than 10% of the recorded prey) items were recorded of nearly 100 species, 33% of which were <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">sparrows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer2_214-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer2-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> When capturing avian prey, the tawny owls not only pluck the prey but also often decapitate and inflict fairly extensive skeletal damage, especially when the victim is a relatively large bird.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> When a population of <a href="/wiki/Great_tit" title="Great tit">great</a> (<i>Parus major</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_blue_tit" title="Eurasian blue tit">Eurasian blue tits</a> (<i>Cyanistes caeruleus</i>) was artificially increased by researchers in the vicinity of tawny owl nests, it was found that, despite the tits not being common food, the owls did reduce the population of increased tit. Many more tits were taken during snow cover or while incubating, with male tits being often being taken in larger numbers, and tit numbers were further reduced when fewer rodents were available.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Gothenburg" title="Gothenburg">Gothenburg</a>, Sweden, on 26 tawny territories the diversity of songbird species was higher (at 12.83 average species) inside tawny territories than outside (10.3 species). Attempts to study whether songbirds were significant in the foods found that bird altogether amounted to 6.78% of the total prey numbers (most likely to be thrushes), so were not significantly effected here.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> As is the case with other medium-sized owls in Europe, there is some evidence that local <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow cover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> habitats and/or <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a> will increase the importance of avian prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> There was a fairly strong indication of local urban habitat causing the tawny owl to take a large quantity of bird prey in <a href="/wiki/Grunewald" title="Grunewald">Grunewald</a>. Here, among 13,359 prey items, bird constituted 35.9% with primary prey of this group being <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrow</a> (<i>Passer domesticus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/European_greenfinch" title="European greenfinch">European greenfinch</a> (<i>Chloris chloris</i>) and that avian prey was more reliable and productive in the area than rodent prey due to the cyclic populations of the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-Wendland2_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland2-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere in Germany, in the <a href="/wiki/Pankow" title="Pankow">Pankow</a> borough of <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, the house sparrow was also the most regular prey species, at 19.2% of 1912 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Schulz_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schulz-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> The diet of tawny owls in the Polish city of <a href="/wiki/Toru%C5%84" title="ToruÅ„">ToruÅ„</a> was also dominated by birds, with them making up 47.8% by number and 51.8% of the biomass against 39.8% of the number and 36.1% of the biomass by mammals. Here the diet was led by the house sparrow (25.9% by number, 22.6% biomass) and secondarily the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_tree_sparrow" title="Eurasian tree sparrow">Eurasian tree sparrow</a> (<i>Passer montanus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Zalewski_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalewski-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, birds were dominant in the city, at 73% of the food. However, much like ToruÅ„, in the rural or outer suburban vicinity (i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Kampinos_Forest" title="Kampinos Forest">Kampinos Forest</a>) outside the urbanized areas of Warsaw, other prey such as rodents and <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a> were favored instead.<sup id="cite_ref-Goszczynski2_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goszczynski2-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grzedzicka_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grzedzicka-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> The urbanization effect was particularly strongly noted in England, where birds constituted only about 4% of the foods in the countryside per two studies, but in <a href="/wiki/Wythenshawe" title="Wythenshawe">Wythenshawe</a> part of <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> and <a href="/wiki/Holland_Park" title="Holland Park">Holland Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, birds made up 89% and 93% of the foods, respectively. The most important avian foods to English tawny owls were the house sparrow (at 27% and 52% in Wythenshawe and Holland Park), the <a href="/wiki/Common_starling" title="Common starling">common starling</a> (<i>Sturnus vulgaris</i>) (at 33% in Wythenshawe) and the <a href="/wiki/Rock_dove" title="Rock dove">rock dove</a> (at 22% in Holland Park).<sup id="cite_ref-Glue_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Some urban pairs in Italy derived about 50% of their food from <a href="/wiki/Rock_dove" title="Rock dove">rock doves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_swift" title="Common swift">common swifts</a> (<i>Apus apus</i>) of all ages, grabbing prey of the two species directly off their building ledge nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti6_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti6-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> of <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, where small native rodents are scarce, birds account for about 39% of the diet tawny owls amongst 2472 prey items, in particular the house and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_sparrow" title="Spanish sparrow">Spanish sparrows</a> (<i>Passer hispaniolensis</i>), at 16.6% by number and 17% by biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Tergou_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tergou-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Birds strongly dominated the foods of tawny owls in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> area, such as northern <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, accounting for more than 70% of 229 prey items, especially <i><a href="/wiki/Passer" title="Passer">Passer</a></i> species.<sup id="cite_ref-Comay2_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comay2-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> In the northern stretches of the range, when birds are taken, slightly larger avian prey such as <a href="/wiki/Thrush_(bird)" title="Thrush (bird)">thrushes</a>, often averaging about 80&#160;g (2.8&#160;oz), tend to be taken instead of sparrows and the like.<sup id="cite_ref-Lundberg_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lundberg-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tishechkin_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tishechkin-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Vogelenzang" title="Vogelenzang">Amsterdamse-Waterleidingduinen</a> area of the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, birds tended to dominate the biomass especially medium-sized passerines such as common starlings and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_jay" title="Eurasian jay">Eurasian jays</a> (<i>Garrulus glandarius</i>), with these contributing 54% of the biomass in high <i>Apodemus</i> mouse years to 62.7% in low mouse years in Marchâ€“May. Smaller birds such as birds decrease from 21.1 to 3.1% in the spring while large birds such as <a href="/wiki/Columba_(genus)" title="Columba (genus)">pigeons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_woodcock" title="Eurasian woodcock">Eurasian woodcocks</a> (<i>Scolopax rusticola</i>) may increase from 16.2 to 37.7% during high and low years for mice.<sup id="cite_ref-Koning2_192-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koning2-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> In a small study from <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, a large portion of diet consisted of birds in summer (61% of the biomass but only 23.2% by number) while, in winter, voles almost completely dominated the foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde4_161-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde4-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg/220px-Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg/330px-Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg/440px-Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3140" data-file-height="2355" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Urban living tawny owls may come to live extensively especially off of sparrows such as <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_tree_sparrow" title="Eurasian tree sparrow">Eurasian tree sparrows</a>, both pictured.</div></div></div>
<p>A huge diversity of birds may be taken by tawny owls, although most are not numerically significant.<sup id="cite_ref-Glue_263-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Slightly over half of the avian prey spectrum for tawny owls are various passerines down to the size of Europe's smallest bird, the 5.2&#160;g (0.18&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Goldcrest" title="Goldcrest">goldcrest</a> (<i>Regulus regulus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glue_263-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> At the other end of the size scale for passerine prey are <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_jay" title="Eurasian jay">jays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_magpie" title="Eurasian magpie">magpies</a> and assorted <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glue_263-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> In some cases, tawny owls have apparently preyed on adult crows of around their own size or slightly larger, such as an estimated 572&#160;g (1.261&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">carrion crow</a> (<i>Corvus corone</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Petty5_242-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty5-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Frequently, the largest prey item found in dietary studies for tawny owls are relatively outsized birds, such as the aforementioned crow, or an estimated 220&#160;g (7.8&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Western_jackdaw" title="Western jackdaw">western jackdaw</a> (<i>Corvus monedula</i>) in central <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a> (<i>Falco tinniculus</i>) of the same estimated weight in <a href="/wiki/Suffolk" title="Suffolk">Suffolk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Petty2_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty2-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Manganaro_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manganaro-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kirk_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirk-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> Although many species of dove are also taken,<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> rock doves and <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">common wood pigeon</a>, the latter taken frequently as adults and estimated to average at 480&#160;g (1.06&#160;lb) when taken in England by two different studies, can be very hearty prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Hardy2_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hardy2-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty5_242-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty5-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Other large avian prey reported taken as adults by tawny owls (many of which approach or exceed the owls themselves in body mass) includes <a href="/wiki/Green-winged_teal" title="Green-winged teal">green-winged teal</a> (<i>Anas crecca</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Petty5_242-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty5-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Red_grouse" title="Red grouse">red grouse</a> (<i>Lagopus lagopus scotica</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Yalden_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yalden-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hazel_grouse" title="Hazel grouse">hazel grouse</a> (<i>Tetrastes bonasia</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Grey_partridge" title="Grey partridge">grey partridge</a> (<i>Perdix perdix</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chukar_partridge" title="Chukar partridge">chukar</a> (<i>Alectoris chukar</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Common_moorhen" title="Common moorhen">common moorhen</a> (<i>Gallinula chloropus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_coot" title="Eurasian coot">Eurasian coot</a> (<i>Fulica atra</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Black-headed_gull" title="Black-headed gull">black-headed gull</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Bergman_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergman-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Black-legged_kittiwake" title="Black-legged kittiwake">black-legged kittiwake</a> (<i>Rissa tridactyla</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Black_woodpecker" title="Black woodpecker">black woodpecker</a> (<i>Dryocopus martius</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Larger <a href="/wiki/Gamebird" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamebird">gamebirds</a> have been taken such as <a href="/wiki/Black_grouse" title="Black grouse">black grouse</a> (<i>Tetrao tetrix</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Common_pheasant" title="Common pheasant">common pheasant</a> (<i>Phasianus colchicus</i>) as well as are some large birds of prey are sometimes found in the foods of tawny owls but it is not clear that these adults and may refer only to juvenile individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> Reportage of tawny owls predation on much larger <a href="/wiki/Western_capercaillie" title="Western capercaillie">western capercaillie</a> (<i>Tetrao urogallus</i>) is quite likely to refer to juvenile capercaillie.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In at least one case, a tawny owl preyed upon an adult <a href="/wiki/Mallard" title="Mallard">mallard</a> (<i>Anas platyrhynchos</i>), which, at a mean weight of around 1,060&#160;g (2.34&#160;lb), is about twice a tawny owl's size and possibly the largest prey known to be tackled by the species.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schnurre_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnurre-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Reptiles.2C_amphibians_and_fish"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reptiles,_amphibians_and_fish">Reptiles, amphibians and fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reptiles, amphibians and fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Frog_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Common_Frog_%281%29.jpg/220px-Common_Frog_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Common_Frog_%281%29.jpg/330px-Common_Frog_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Common_Frog_%281%29.jpg/440px-Common_Frog_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2264" data-file-height="1698" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Frog_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">Frogs</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Common_frog" title="Common frog">common frogs</a> are a common component of the tawny owl's diet.</div></div></div>
<p>Little evidence has been found of tawny owl predation on <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>. Despite their scarcity about a dozen species are known to be hunted by this predator, including a couple species of <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snake</a> and several <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizard</a> species.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hirons2_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons2-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Comay_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comay-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alegre_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alegre-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> They are more or less taken incidentally, constituting always less than 2% of the foods in known European studies.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gryz3_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gryz3-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> An exceptional case was in Sahel, Algeria, where the <a href="/wiki/Tarentola_mauritanica" title="Tarentola mauritanica">Moorish gecko</a> (<i>Tarentola mauritanica</i>) was the leading prey species at 16.75% of 2472 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Tergou_228-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tergou-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">Amphibians</a> are generally much more prominent in the tawny owl's diet, almost exclusively <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Nearly 20 species of amphibian are known to be taken, which includes two species of <a href="/wiki/Newt" title="Newt">newt</a> outside of the more typical frogs and <a href="/wiki/Toad" title="Toad">toads</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tergou_228-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tergou-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arslangundogdu_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arslangundogdu-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Key to predation on frogs is the composition of the habitat, with frogs and toads being apparently much more accessible in remote and conserved areas rather than developed lands.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In different areas of Poland, the <i><a href="/wiki/Rana_(genus)" title="Rana (genus)">Rana</a></i> genus of frogs led the prey composition such as in <a href="/wiki/Bia%C5%82owie%C5%BCa_Forest" title="BiaÅ‚owieÅ¼a Forest">BiaÅ‚owieÅ¼a Forest</a>, where they made up 21.1% of the foods, in <a href="/wiki/Wigry_National_Park" title="Wigry National Park">Wigry National Park</a> where they constituted 17.4% of the diet and in the northeasterly section of the country where they made up 23.4% of 2046 vertebrate prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_269-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gryz4_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gryz4-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lesinski2_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lesinski2-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere frogs and amphibians are regular but secondary foods. Of 3194 prey items in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, amphibians (probably all frogs) were secondary to rodents, and could account for from 16.6% in good vole years to 19.5%, the leading prey type by number, in bad vole years, with an average 17.5%. from all years.<sup id="cite_ref-Karhunen_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karhunen-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, frogs constituted 14.5% of 1125 prey items, with the <a href="/wiki/Common_frog" title="Common frog">common frog</a> (<i>Rana temporaria</i>) in particular accounting  for 11.2%.<sup id="cite_ref-Balciauskiene_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balciauskiene-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> In Sahel, Algeria, the <a href="/wiki/Discoglossus_pictus" title="Discoglossus pictus">Mediterranean painted frog</a> (<i>Discoglossus pictus</i>) was a fairly important prey resource, at 9.22% by number and 10.5% by biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Tergou_228-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tergou-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> The average size of frogs taken can be fairly variable, from an estimated 7 to 39.8&#160;g (0.25 to 1.40&#160;oz), as claimed for central Italy and England, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Capizzi_188-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capizzi-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty2_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty2-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> There are now several instances known of tawny owls preying on <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, though they are not known to be a significant food source anywhere in the distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> About eight species of wild fish are known to have been captured, including probably young or infirm specimens of large fish such as <a href="/wiki/Northern_pike" title="Northern pike">northern pike</a> (<i>Esox lucius</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Brown_trout" title="Brown trout">brown trout</a> (<i>Salmo trutta</i>), with at least some instances of tawny owls also catching ornamental <a href="/wiki/Goldfish" title="Goldfish">goldfish</a> (<i>Carassius auratus</i>) as well.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty5_242-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty5-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wiacek_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiacek-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrates">Invertebrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Invertebrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The tawny owl feeds more extensively on <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> than many of the more northerly European studies would indicate.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Virtually, any variety of edible invertebrate would be eaten by these owls, though generally <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> are taken due to the high occurrence of encounters. Nearly 70 invertebrate prey species have been noted.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In the more southerly parts of Europe, much stronger numbers of invertebrates tend to be detected.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In central Italy, invertebrates constituted 53.3% of 654 prey items, in particular <a href="/wiki/Scarabaeidae" title="Scarabaeidae">scarab beetles</a> (15.8%), <a href="/wiki/Stylommatophora" title="Stylommatophora">land snails</a> (12.1%), <a href="/wiki/Ground_beetle" title="Ground beetle">ground beetles</a> (5.3%), <a href="/wiki/Orthoptera" title="Orthoptera">Orthoptera</a> (5.14%) and <a href="/wiki/Longhorn_beetle" title="Longhorn beetle">longhorn beetles</a> (5%).<sup id="cite_ref-Manganaro_266-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manganaro-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> In the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Umbria" title="Umbria">Umbria</a> in Italy, 47.8% of 874 prey items were invertebrates, particularly <a href="/wiki/Limacidae" title="Limacidae">keelback slugs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arionidae" title="Arionidae">roundback slugs</a>, which together made up 28.6% of the prey items followed among invertebrates by <a href="/wiki/Melolonthinae" title="Melolonthinae">Melolonthinae</a> subfamily of scarab beetles at about 7% of the prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaggi_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaggi-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Mur%C3%A1nska_planina_National_Park" title="MurÃ¡nska planina National Park">MurÃ¡nska planina National Park</a> in Slovakia, keelback slugs were the most identified prey type among 13,912 prey items, accounting for 26.1%.<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch4_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch4-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> Keelback slugs were also the main prey type in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> (accounting for 24.6% of 529 prey items), and detected in strong numbers in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a> (wherein they made up 21.8% of 514 prey items, but trailed by number the yellow-necked mouse).<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> A strong dominance of insect prey was detected in food studies from Spain, with 64.3% of 1002 prey items from across the nation being invertebrates. In the Spanish region, the <a href="/wiki/Wood_mouse" title="Wood mouse">wood mouse</a> was the identified single prey species (at 20% by number and 21% by biomass) but was closely followed by <a href="/wiki/Tettigoniidae" title="Tettigoniidae">bush crickets</a> (at 19.76% and 1.5% biomass), <i><a href="/wiki/Rhizotrogus_aestivus" title="Rhizotrogus aestivus">Rhizotrogus aestivus</a></i> (10.76% by number, 0.5% biomass), <a href="/wiki/Gryllus_campestris" title="Gryllus campestris">European field cricket</a> (<i>Gryllus campestris</i>) (8.85% by number, 1% biomass), <a href="/wiki/Typhaeus_typhoeus" title="Typhaeus typhoeus">minotaur beetle</a> (<i>Typhaeus typhoeus</i>) (7.92% #, 1% biomass) and <a href="/wiki/Anoplotrupes_stercorosus" title="Anoplotrupes stercorosus">common dor beetle</a> (<i>Anoplotrupes stercorosus</i>) (4.35% by number, 1% biomass).<sup id="cite_ref-Adanez_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adanez-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> More locally within the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Province of LeÃ³n">Province of LeÃ³n</a>, beetles collectively constituted 35.1% of the diet and Orthoptera constituted 14.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-Alegre_273-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alegre-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> In Sahel, Algeria, invertebrates in total slightly outnumbered mammals, but lagged slightly behind birds in number.<sup id="cite_ref-Tergou_228-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tergou-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> In general, insects in central and northern Europe are a regular but secondary food source, taken in similar volume to birds but far less significant as contributors to biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_168-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls are said to take beetles in central Europe more frequently in Aprilâ€“May before ground cover becomes too extensive.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> An exceptional case of invertebrates being primary as a food source in a northerly country was recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Peak_District" title="Peak District">Peak District</a> of England, wherein <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a> were the primary food type, at 40.4% of 926 prey items and 15.6% of the biomass. Strong numbers were detected here too of <i><a href="/wiki/Geotrupes" title="Geotrupes">Geotrupes</a></i> beetles, contributing 14% of the prey numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-Yalden_268-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yalden-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecies_predatory_relationships">Interspecies predatory relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Interspecies predatory relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chouettes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Chouettes.jpg/220px-Chouettes.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Chouettes.jpg/330px-Chouettes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Chouettes.jpg/440px-Chouettes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="1880" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chouettes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A captive tawny owl with a large <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a>, usually the tawny owl is behaviourly dominant and slightly larger than the barn owl.</div></div></div>
<p>In every part of its range, tawny owls co-exist with other <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>, with other owls presenting the strongest possibility for competition given their overlapping food selection and shared <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnality</a>. As perhaps the most numerous and one of the most widely distributed in the continent of the 13 owl species regularly occurring in Europe, ecological interactions of some kind have been recorded with most other species.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Given their medium-sized frame and general adaptability, of special interest is how they co-exist with other medium-sized owls such as <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Many studies have contrasted particularly the food habits of long-eared owls living in proximity to tawny owls. Generally speaking, the long-eared owls in Europe are much more strongly disposed to being a <a href="/wiki/Generalist_and_specialist_species" title="Generalist and specialist species">specialist species</a> than the tawny owl, relying almost entirely on <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>. Although in the broad picture, the long-eared also feeds on other prey such as birds and insects, their food niche breadth is consistently lower than that of the tawny owls. For example, in a very large study of central Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Common_vole" title="Common vole">common vole</a> species alone constituted about 76% of 57,500 prey items for long-eared owls.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Balciauskas_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balciauskas-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Balciauskiene2_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balciauskiene2-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nilsson3_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nilsson3-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> Long-eared owls also differ strongly from tawny owls in selecting much more open hunting grounds, such as old <a href="/wiki/Field_(agriculture)" title="Field (agriculture)">fields</a>, usually hunting on the wing rather than from a perch and in utilizing abandoned (and often rather open) bird nests rather than natural cavities as nesting sites.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> In terms of <a href="/wiki/Peri-urbanisation" title="Peri-urbanisation">peri-urbanisation</a>, the long-eared and tawny owls are more or less equally adaptive to such areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The food niche breadth is usually greater in Europe for the tawny owl than for the barn owl as well,<sup id="cite_ref-Tergou_228-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tergou-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nedyalkov_237-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nedyalkov-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> although the barn owl appears to have a stronger liking of shrews as prey than does the tawny owl (shrews more than twice as often selected).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The barn owl, although also by nature a cavity nester, does not generally acclimate to well-wooded areas where the tawny owl is at home.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Both the long-eared and barn owls prefer voles where they are available, especially as both often hunt in open areas where they are common, whereas <i>Apodemus</i> mice tend to be slightly preferred by tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Erlinge_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erlinge-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> When conflicts ensue, the tawny owl tends to dominate these other medium-sized owls. This is in part due to their size advantage, with the tawny being larger by an average of 32% (of 3 standards of measurements, two from the wing, one by body mass) than the long-eared owl and 24% larger than the barn owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls are known to readily evict barn owls from their own nest sites, normally when taking up residency in towns or cities.<sup id="cite_ref-Glutz_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glutz-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> It was additionally found that barn owls, being a species better adapted to warmer, more tropical areas, is at higher risk of starvation in cool weather than long-eared and tawny owls, with proportionately many more found dead in winter in France due presumably to inferior <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_metabolism" title="Fatty acid metabolism">lipid fat reserves</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Massemin_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Massemin-289">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>, the tawny owl is the largest and most powerful year-around native owl, ahead slightly of the long-eared and barn owls. Therefore, the tawny owl may be considered an <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predator</a> here (despite still being vulnerable occasionally to diurnal raptors and ground predators). However, since the tawny owl never colonized <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, here the long-eared owl is the largest year-around owl (in these island much larger owls are very rare winter visitors, in the case of the <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owl</a> (<i>Bubo scandiacus</i>) or are probably accidentally introduced by humans, as is likely the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Crossley_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crossley-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yalden_268-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yalden-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> However, in much of mainland Europe and elsewhere, tawny owls potentially overlap with larger owls and, depending on habitat composition and prey accessibilities, may be considered more correctly a <a href="/wiki/Mesopredator" title="Mesopredator">mesopredator</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Mainly in parts of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">northern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">eastern Europe</a>, tawny owls sometimes co-exist with their larger cousins, <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owls</a>. The Ural owl has generally similar nesting and feeding habits but tends to occur in slightly different habitats. Generally, the Ural is more broadly adaptive to <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> and similarly conifer based forests than are tawny owls and is also somewhat more likely to be active during daylight.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Korpimaki_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korpimaki-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lundberg_217-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lundberg-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>  In eastern Europe, the Ural species tends to occur more so at higher elevation in montane forest such as the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian Mountains</a>, especially those with extensive old growth of <a href="/wiki/Beech" title="Beech">beech</a> trees, while the tawny owls tend to occur at lower elevations and more mixed forest with fewer <a href="/w/index.php?title=Glade_(ecology)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Glade (ecology) (page does not exist)">glades</a> in these areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec2_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec2-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pacenovsky_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pacenovsky-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> A relatively low territory overlap of 13.3% was detected in Slovakia between tawny and Ural owl territories due to their differing habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> Depending on range, the prey sizes taken by tawny owls tend to be considerably smaller than those selected by the more powerful Ural owl, with the latter's mean prey sizes averaging from 31 to 50% larger. However, the food niche breadth is up to two and a half times greater in the tawny than in the Ural owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Korpimaki_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korpimaki-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tishechkin_265-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tishechkin-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> The Ural owl tends to dominate interspecific conflicts with tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Korpimaki_135-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korpimaki-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tishechkin_265-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tishechkin-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pacenovsky_291-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pacenovsky-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> On the contrary, in at least one case a tawny owl was observed to fiercely attack and drive off a Ural owl (although it may not be ruled out that this was a case of <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_(animal_behavior)" title="Mobbing (animal behavior)">mobbing</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec2_290-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec2-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pacenovsky_291-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pacenovsky-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> A third and much larger still <i>Strix</i> species, the <a href="/wiki/Great_grey_owl" title="Great grey owl">great grey owl</a>, differs considerably in almost all respects of its life history from tawny owls. The great grey is adapted to taiga and other conifer based forests, both open and enclosed, and relies almost exclusively on voles for food. Almost <a href="/wiki/Cathemerality" title="Cathemerality">cathemeral</a> in activity, the great grey species may nest in a broad variety of situations in the boreal habitat but never utilizes tree cavities as does the tawny. Due to the latter species' specialization, the tawny owl is spared from any known ecological interactions with the great grey owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Korpimaki_135-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korpimaki-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tishechkin_265-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tishechkin-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Much more dangerous than larger <i>Strix</i> species to the tawny owl is the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a>. A similar wide-ranging generalist, the eagle owl most often nests in and around rock formations, often in fairly mountainous areas, but locally is also adaptive to varying habitats and may too nesting in old birds nests or on the ground, usually between the trunks of large trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> In terms of their dietary habits, the eagle owl appears to be perhaps an even more indiscriminate predator, attacking animals of all taxonomic classes unfortunate enough to encounter them.
