Given its far larger size and much more powerful features, the eagle owl will attack much larger prey than tawny owls, even relative to their own size.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_190-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani_294-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls are likely to avoid encounters with eagle-owls and are fortunate in many areas that the eagle-owl, which requires a larger home range and tends to more exclusively prefer remote areas than tawnys, can be scarce to absent in some parts of Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani_294-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> In some areas of Spain and Italy, tawny owls have adapted to live in the vicinity of wooded montane areas and even to nesting within rock formations. Both countries have healthy recovered populations of eagle-owls, so tawny owls appear to locally restrict their vocal activity and tend to occur on the fringes or outside active eagle-owl ranges.<sup id="cite_ref-Sanchez-Zapata_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-Zapata-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lourenco_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lourenco-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> Unlike their larger, more powerful cousin, the Ural owl, the tawny owl is not infrequently vicim to predation by larger raptors.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup>  There are at least 300 recorded instances of predation on tawny owls in Europe by Eurasian eagle-owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_298-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> They tend to be taken somewhat less than other medium-sized owls, especially long-eared owls, by eagle owls by virtue of using woodlands (which differ somewhat from the habitats usually used by eagle-owls) and nesting in tree hollows.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_298-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> The other greatest predatory threat is certain to be the <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a> (<i>Accipiter gentilis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Koning_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koning-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_298-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> There are at least a hundred cases of goshawks taking the owls and, unlike the eagle-owl, the habitats of the goshawks do fairly closely mirror those of tawny owls with the owls only spared by its different primary times of activity.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_298-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other predators long known to have taken tawny owls have included their larger cousins, the <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owls</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzards</a> (<i>Buteo buteo</i>), <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">red kites</a> (<i>Milvus milvus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcons</a> (<i>Falco peregrinus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_298-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> In addition, more reported raptorial predators have included the <a href="/wiki/Bonelli%27s_eagle" title="Bonelli&#39;s eagle">Bonelli's eagle</a> (<i>Aquila fasciata</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Resano_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano-301">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> (<i>Aquila chrysaetos</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagle</a> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-303">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kite</a> (<i>Milvus migrans</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> Outside of traditionally raptorial groups, birds such as <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a> may destroy and/or compromise tawny owl nests, either for food, anti-predator behaviour and/or competition. <a href="/wiki/Western_jackdaw" title="Western jackdaw">Western jackdaws</a>, in particular, appear to be persistent competitors for nest sites and sometimes are aggressive enough as to displace tawny owls from a disputed site. In extreme cases of competition with jackdaws, the owls may bring themselves to starvation trying to incubate their nests in the hole when a <a href="/wiki/Sociality" title="Sociality">murder</a> of jackdaws continuously visit, harass and place a new nest on top of the owl's eggs repeatedly. In other cases, the owls nestlings have been suffocated by the jackdaws building a nest directly on top of the still living owl broods.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Koning_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koning-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> Mammalian predators are a fairly frequent threat to tawny owls as well, though tend to attack almost exclusively during the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_pine_marten" title="European pine marten">European pine martens</a> are known to be a considerable threat of all aged tawny owls at nests from nestlings to brooding females, as are probably <a href="/wiki/Stone_marten" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone marten">stone martens</a> (<i>Martes foina</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sunde5_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde5-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> In a food study in France, 9% of the diet of pine martens was found to consist of tawny owls, with the data indicating that owls using nest boxes are more vulnerable to martens.<sup id="cite_ref-Baudvin3_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baudvin3-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> Especially once reaching or around the age of fledging, <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red foxes</a> (<i>Vulpes vulpes</i>) are known to take several young tawny owls (and perhaps an unwary adult), at times taking up to 39% of young owls in a population, as are probably <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a> (<i>Felis silvestris</i>) in some areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sunde5_308-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde5-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> However, in chance encounters during the day, tawny owls have been known to attack and successfully chase off pine martens and have been seen to do the same to red foxes, cats and dogs.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Szomjas_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Szomjas-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The tawny owl is a considerable predator itself of smaller owls. Data indicates that it is second deadliest owl to the smaller species of owl in Europe, behind only the eagle owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Among their known owl prey species are <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_scops_owl" title="Eurasian scops owl">Eurasian scops owls</a> (<i>Otus scops</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Obuch_187-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obuch-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_pygmy_owl" title="Eurasian pygmy owl">Eurasian pygmy owls</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Overskaug_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overskaug-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owls</a> (<i>Athene noctua</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owls</a><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Boreal_owl" title="Boreal owl">boreal owls</a> (<i>Aegolius funereus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_298-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, they may hunt smaller diurnal birds of prey such as <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawks</a> (<i>Accipiter nisus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrels</a> (<i>Falco tinnunculus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Kirk_267-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirk-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_hobby" title="Eurasian hobby">Eurasian hobbys</a> (<i>Falco subbuteo</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(bird)" title="Merlin (bird)">merlins</a> (<i>Falco columbarius</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_298-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> Reports of tawny owls killing common buzzards and northern goshawks are of nebulous detail and may refer in fact to nighttime nest robberies rather than overpowering adults of these larger, dangerous and often seemingly avoided raptors.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Evidence from Slovenia has indicated that the tawny owl is more feared by small owls such as the boreal owl than even the larger, more powerful Ural owl, as they clustered more strongly as can be explained by habitat in the realm of Ural owl territories but seemed to avoid where possible tawny owl territories.<sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec2_290-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec2-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zlender_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zlender-312">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> Although there are more known instances of tawny owls hunting little owls, data in central Europe could not distinguish whether little owls were avoiding tawny owls or the wooded habitats they frequent to account for their sometimes spotty ranges.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> However, when forced to nest in quite close proximity to tawny owls and other medium-sized owl species due to clustered "islands" of habitat remaining in southeastern Poland, the productivity of little owls appeared to lower.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> Predation by tawny owls can be severe as well on <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_pygmy_owl" title="Eurasian pygmy owl">Eurasian pygmy owls</a>, to such an extent that they may have cause the <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">regional extinction</a> of the pygmy around <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Forest" title="Black Forest">Black Forest</a> after the smaller species was already depleted by <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>. A successful reintroduction of Eurasian pygmys into the forest was followed by a natural range expansion back into the forest by tawnys, which again threatens the population growth of smaller owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig2_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig2-315">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Owls_(5)_(8403003812).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Tawny_Owls_%285%29_%288403003812%29.jpg/220px-Tawny_Owls_%285%29_%288403003812%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Tawny_Owls_%285%29_%288403003812%29.jpg/330px-Tawny_Owls_%285%29_%288403003812%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Tawny_Owls_%285%29_%288403003812%29.jpg/440px-Tawny_Owls_%285%29_%288403003812%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Owls_(5)_(8403003812).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A nestling tawny owl looks out from a nest box.</div></div></div>
<p>Tawny owls are <a href="/wiki/Monogamous" class="mw-redirect" title="Monogamous">monogamous</a> and territorial year around. Young birds select territories and look for mates in autumn and tend to be very vocal, especially males.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Due to their highly territorial behaviour, young birds frequently struggle to establish a territory unless a nearby adult dies. Males routinely engage in territorial fights.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Territories have been known to have been maintained by single tawnys for up to 10 years in Russia and 13 years in Berlin.<sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_110-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Of 34 males in Wytham, only one male moved off of territory, due to being disturbed by humans.<sup id="cite_ref-Hirons_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> It appears to be largely up to the male to select territorial boundaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Despite the aforementioned territorial behaviour, active nests of two separate pairs at as close as 100&#160;m (330&#160;ft), in the <a href="/wiki/Tegel" title="Tegel">Tegel forest</a>, have been reported.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This species shows very little extrapair parentage.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> In Switzerland for example, a study of 137 nestings found that only one, or 0.7%, were from a different father than the mate, females cannot generally raise young without male contribution so the pair structure of these highly residential owls insures little instance of <a href="/wiki/Cuckoldry" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuckoldry">cuckoldry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> Cases of <a href="/wiki/Bigamy" title="Bigamy">bigamy</a> were reported at <a href="/wiki/Wytham" title="Wytham">Wytham</a> in 6 of 34 males, in situations where apparently a neighboring male died and was suffixed subsequently, however, one or the other nesting attempts would completely fail each time.<sup id="cite_ref-Hirons_45-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hirons4_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons4-318">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Pavia" title="Pavia">Pavia</a>, 3 of 22 territories included two mature females.<sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti4_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti4-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nests">Nests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Nests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nistkasten_f%C3%BCr_den_Waldkauz_(Strix_aluco).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nistkasten_f%C3%BCr_den_Waldkauz_%28Strix_aluco%29.JPG/220px-Nistkasten_f%C3%BCr_den_Waldkauz_%28Strix_aluco%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nistkasten_f%C3%BCr_den_Waldkauz_%28Strix_aluco%29.JPG/330px-Nistkasten_f%C3%BCr_den_Waldkauz_%28Strix_aluco%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nistkasten_f%C3%BCr_den_Waldkauz_%28Strix_aluco%29.JPG/440px-Nistkasten_f%C3%BCr_den_Waldkauz_%28Strix_aluco%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3872" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nistkasten_f%C3%BCr_den_Waldkauz_(Strix_aluco).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Nest_box" title="Nest box">nest box</a> set out for tawny owls.</div></div></div>
<p>The male advertises several potential <a href="/wiki/Nest" title="Nest">nest</a> sites to his mate by singing at the entrance, slipping inside and so on, with the female finally selecting one.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The typical nest site of a tawny owl is a <a href="/wiki/Tree_hollow" title="Tree hollow">tree hollow</a>, wherein the owls will nest directly on the interior hole's surface. Tree hollows used may be as much as 25&#160;m (82&#160;ft) above the ground, but are usually within about 12&#160;m (39&#160;ft) of the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Virtually any species of <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a> tree may be used provided holes are available. These tree cavities may be of any origin, with trees that grow large such as <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beech" title="Beech">beech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maple" title="Maple">maple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tilia" title="Tilia">lime</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hornbeam" title="Hornbeam">hornbeam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alder" title="Alder">alder</a> often regularly utilized.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiacek_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiacek-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> Female may scratch out a shallow base in soil if present and sometimes seen to reportedly tear eggs into pieces as a cushion from their broods.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Othe tree nest locations have included those on top of a <a href="/wiki/Witch%27s_broom" title="Witch&#39;s broom">Witch's broom</a> and on top of the tree <a href="/wiki/Canopy_(biology)" title="Canopy (biology)">canopy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mebs_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mebs-322">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> Natural holes in trees are often the most frequently used nesting site, followed closely in recent decades many artificial <a href="/wiki/Nest_box" title="Nest box">nest boxes</a>, preferably those with a 15&#160;cm Ã—&#160;20&#160;cm (5.9&#160;in Ã—&#160;7.9&#160;in) entrance or larger.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> Of the nest boxes erected in different parts from Kielder forest and the <a href="/wiki/Glenbranter" title="Glenbranter">Glenbranter forest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Argyll" title="Argyll">Argyll</a>, 592 nest boxes were placed at 1.6 to 5.2&#160;m (5.2 to 17.1&#160;ft) high along the side of trees and 17.4% of which were used by tawnys (in latter 2 years of study up to 24.1%).<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> In southeastern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, all nest boxes erected in habitat were eventually utilized by tawny owls.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> Many nest boxes were recorded to be used as roost sites in the <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a> area, with only 12.3% of the 44% of nest boxes actually used by owls for breeding, usually with the owls utilizing boxes that were at least 6&#160;m (20&#160;ft) above the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> Nest boxes are most successful wherever natural tree cavities are scarce or absent, such as conifer forests, young successional woods and farmlands.<sup id="cite_ref-Petty3_206-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty3-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Saurola_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saurola-327">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Plesnik_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plesnik-328">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owls may not infrequently nest in an unmodified <a href="/wiki/Black_woodpecker" title="Black woodpecker">black woodpecker</a> hole.<sup id="cite_ref-Wille_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wille-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> This species may too nest in nest of larger birds such as <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common ravens</a> (<i>Corvus corax</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_magpie" title="Eurasian magpie">Eurasian magpies</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawks</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a> while the sometimes recorded as used smaller nests such as those of <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_jay" title="Eurasian jay">Eurasian jays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">common wood pigeons</a> but these are at potential risk of collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>  Occasionally, tawny owls have also been recorded nesting in abandoned burrows of larger mammals (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red fox</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_badger" title="European badger">European badger</a> (<i>Meles meles</i>) as well as those of <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Other nesting locations recorded for the species have included bare <a href="/wiki/Cliff" title="Cliff">cliff ledges</a>, between the <a href="/wiki/Root" title="Root">roots</a> of heavy tree trunks, on the bare <a href="/wiki/Forest_floor" title="Forest floor">forest floor</a> and among <a href="/wiki/Calluna" title="Calluna">heather</a>. Also rarer still nests have been reported in the recesses of stone walls, in <a href="/wiki/Chimney" title="Chimney">chimneys</a> of large <a href="/wiki/Building" title="Building">buildings</a>, on <a href="/wiki/Cottage" title="Cottage">cabins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shed" title="Shed">sheds</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Dovecote" title="Dovecote">dovecotes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steeple" title="Steeple">church towers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In southern Finland, 95% of 123 nest boxes put out were occupied by tawny owls in 1970â€“1975, natural tree hole use decreased in same period from 48% to 3%, on top of stumps from 4% to 1% and in buildings from 15% to 1%.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs">Eggs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Eggs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_MWNH_2201.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Strix_aluco_MWNH_2201.JPG/220px-Strix_aluco_MWNH_2201.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Strix_aluco_MWNH_2201.JPG/330px-Strix_aluco_MWNH_2201.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Strix_aluco_MWNH_2201.JPG/440px-Strix_aluco_MWNH_2201.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2324" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_MWNH_2201.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg, Collection <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden,_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Museum Wiesbaden, Germany">Museum Wiesbaden, Germany</a></div></div></div>
<p>Laying normally begins in March-early April, sometimes as early as February.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Of 344 nests in <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>, the mean egg laying dates were March 13 to April 3, with extreme dates of February 20 to April 30, with shifts accounted for by late winter weather.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> In the German area of <a href="/wiki/Swabian_Jura" title="Swabian Jura">Swabian Jura</a> clutches may start to be laid as early as mid-February if food is favorable.<sup id="cite_ref-Rockenbauch_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockenbauch-330">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> Egg laying in mid-February is fairly typical in Italy as well.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The first egg is typically laid by mid-March in Great Britain, in late March in continental Europe and early April in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In very snowy years in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, the laying of clutch is often either delayed or does not occur at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Linkola_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Linkola-331">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup> Due to the warmer average ambient temperatures and access to <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">pestilent rodents</a>, wintertime egg laying in human developed areas is now known to be commonplace: with records of laying in late December in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, early January in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> and late January in <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rockenbauch_330-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockenbauch-330">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> They tend to lay their clutches earlier than <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owls</a> and much earlier than <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> One clutch is laid per year but rarely replacement clutches have been reported if an entire clutch is lost.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> A record in <a href="/wiki/Bizkaia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bizkaia">Bizkaia</a>, Spain of a second successful clutch was produced, with pairs of owlets were recorded in February then another pair of owlets apparently hatched to the same pair was recorded in July of same year.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> The female typically lays a relatively small clutch of 3-5 eggs, sometimes in extreme case from 2 and very rarely and in the times of food plenty, 7â€“9.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Average clutch sizes were cited as 2.67 in Britain, 2.8 in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, 2.95 in Belgium 3.29 in central Europe, 3.3 in both Switzerland and the Netherlands, 3.65 in Denmark, 3.64-3.81 in Finland and 4.04 in Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hirons_45-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dambiermont_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dambiermont-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Plesnik_328-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plesnik-328">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Solonen2_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solonen2-335">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bos_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bos-336">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup> While in Bohemia, the clutch size varied remarkably little by year, in western Switzerland it could go from 2.52 to 3.63 in different years and Finnish data indicates it can vary from 3.1 to 4.2 in low and peak vole years. Therefore, clutch size may be more variable in more cool climatic zones but, on the other hand, the average size of the clutches averages larger in these colder areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Plesnik_328-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plesnik-328">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Solonen2_335-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solonen2-335">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Solonen3_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solonen3-337">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup> Although clutch sizes average smaller in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> than in mainland Europe, they are more consistently laid there than the clutches of barn owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Their eggs are pure white, smooth or slightly glossy in texture, and vary little in size. Egg dimensions were found to average 46.7&#160;mm Ã—&#160;39.1&#160;mm (1.84&#160;in Ã—&#160;1.54&#160;in) in Britain, 47.6&#160;mm Ã—&#160;39.2&#160;mm (1.87&#160;in Ã—&#160;1.54&#160;in) in central Europe, 46.6&#160;mm Ã—&#160;38.5&#160;mm (1.83&#160;in Ã—&#160;1.52&#160;in) in Sweden (Makatsch 1976) and 47.5&#160;mm Ã—&#160;39.2&#160;mm (1.87&#160;in Ã—&#160;1.54&#160;in) in Russia. The range may be from 42 to 50&#160;mm (1.7 to 2.0&#160;in) in height by 36 to 41&#160;mm (1.4 to 1.6&#160;in) in diameter (sample size 100).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_110-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> The average weight of eggs can vary from 31.3 to 40&#160;g (1.10 to 1.41&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parental_behaviour">Parental behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Parental behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The female <a href="/wiki/Egg_incubation" title="Egg incubation">incubates</a> alone, starting with the first egg for 28â€“29 days typically, and is fed by the male.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The female also broods the chicks, feeding them strips of meat for about 2 weeks, after which they may start swallowing mice and such whole. She continues to feed them until they leave the nest, at which point she may resume her hunting but the male may continue to a majority of food capture and may even feed the offspring directly.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Larger prey items tend to be more often fed to chicks such as medium-sized birds, young rabbits and moles where available, while the parents themselves usually eat small rodents (i.e. in Wytham and <a href="/wiki/River_Ythan" title="River Ythan">Ythan Valley</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Hardy3_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hardy3-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup> Sometimes when the mother feeds their young, separated feathers are sometimes doled out and consumed by them.<sup id="cite_ref-Beven_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beven-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> In France, 4â€“19 prey deliveries by the male were recorded per night, or 2.5â€“3 prey per chick nightly.<sup id="cite_ref-Baudvin2_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baudvin2-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> In Belgium, from as little as 0 to 22 prey items were recorded to be delivered per night, on average 3.7 (range 2.3â€“5.1) prey per chick nightly.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup> Up to 21% of prey deliveries were done in daylight in Wytham whereas in the Netherlands only 2% were during daytime.<sup id="cite_ref-Petty_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup> In one British nest, with three owlets (the oldest being 15â€“25 days old) each chick received about 88&#160;g (3.1&#160;oz) of food per night while later near fledging, each would get 124 to 141&#160;g (4.4 to 5.0&#160;oz) per night.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritter_343-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritter-343">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup> In an experiment study, broods were both increased and decreased to see if males differed their food deliveries, it was found that the males did change their food delivers as a result of brood increase or decrease, with the decreased broods being fed well while the increased broods were food stressed and showed particular signs of declining condition of the brooding female as she had less food for herself.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Intrinsic_and_extrinsic_properties" title="Intrinsic and extrinsic properties">Instrinic factors</a> were weighed about the condition of parents in a study from <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, with the female's age being the most significant factors that could be determined. Furthermore, it was found that male condition was the most important factor for female condition, with older males often in better physical condition.<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup> Weather conditions were found to be intrinsic with condition of parents in Finland, and further that larger females could breed earlier and more successfully on average.<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> A study of <a href="/wiki/Steroid_hormone" title="Steroid hormone">steroid hormones</a> in a selected 51 pairs within Denmark (from a network of 204 nest boxes) were used to measure hormone levels. It was found that testostrone levels were consistently higher in 3 year old birds of both sexes, birds of this age were more productive in all aspects of breeding than younger owls, although it was not clear to what extent higher breeding success to attributable to hormonal levels versus experience.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> A testing of <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">testosterone levels</a> in owl offspring within <a href="/wiki/Danube-Ipoly_National_Park" title="Danube-Ipoly National Park">Danube-Ipoly National Park</a> in Hungary determined that experienced parents produced nestlings with more testostrone mainly in correlation with mild weather. In poor weather or inexperienced parents the levels were lower. The testostrone levels in the Hungarian study were found to be lowest in poor weather conditions in the last of the owl's broods and most such young usually died during the early nesting period.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Owl_(Strix_aluco),_Parc_de_Woluw%C3%A9,_Bruxelles_(23814972790).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_Parc_de_Woluw%C3%A9%2C_Bruxelles_%2823814972790%29.jpg/220px-Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_Parc_de_Woluw%C3%A9%2C_Bruxelles_%2823814972790%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_Parc_de_Woluw%C3%A9%2C_Bruxelles_%2823814972790%29.jpg/330px-Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_Parc_de_Woluw%C3%A9%2C_Bruxelles_%2823814972790%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_Parc_de_Woluw%C3%A9%2C_Bruxelles_%2823814972790%29.jpg/440px-Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29%2C_Parc_de_Woluw%C3%A9%2C_Bruxelles_%2823814972790%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2047" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Owl_(Strix_aluco),_Parc_de_Woluw%C3%A9,_Bruxelles_(23814972790).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny owl peers from a tree hollow, which is often what they naturally utilize as nest sites</div></div></div>
<p>When they are alarmed or required to act in self-defense near the nest, tawny owls of all ages but especially older nestlings up to fledging age, have been recorded to raise feathers and outstretch wings, to their expand size and possible shield itself, also snapping their bills.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> Despite the tawny owl's reputation as a highly aggressive bird when encroached upon,<sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> the parents are usually retiring even when the area of the nest is broached.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Passive measures are usually taken first in parental nest defense.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The mother owls are often tight sitters, one female sat motionless even as she was physically turned over, while another sat tight but flushed eventually after several human intrusions.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup> Sometimes parents engage in <a href="/wiki/Distraction_display" title="Distraction display">distraction display</a> when attempting to protect their young but less frequently than do long-eared owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Rockenbauch_330-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockenbauch-330">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> There is much individual variation in aggressiveness of response to disturbance and threats, with a similar occasional but widely reported tendencies for aggressiveness in nest defense in many other <i>Strix</i> owls as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In the earlier part of the nestling period, males sometimes launch defensive attacks but these tend to be relatively mild and no physical contact is often made.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In western Switzerland, females were 2.9 times more likely to respond aggressively to artificial nest predator stimuli than males.<sup id="cite_ref-DaSilva_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DaSilva-352">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> Later into the nesting period, the female may begin showing more aggressive reactions to disturbance or threats.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Older females were found in a study show a more aggressive defense as well as those with larger broods and earlier nestings, however non-aggressive females were found to have more future reproductive years on average than aggressive ones.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wallin_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallin-353">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup> Rufous morph females were more vigorous in nest defense than other morphs in a study from Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-DaSilva_352-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DaSilva-352">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> At the first detection of an intruder, the female may let out muffled hoots initially.<sup id="cite_ref-Delmee_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delmee-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Attacks only tend to occur somewhat regularly in developed areas, especially city parks, where repeated intrusions occur and perhaps resulting <a href="/wiki/Desensitization_(psychology)" title="Desensitization (psychology)">desensitization</a> and irritation.<sup id="cite_ref-Southern_44-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Some individual females and rarely males become routine attackers on humans and may be known as "furies".<sup id="cite_ref-Dambiermont_157-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dambiermont-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wallin_353-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallin-353">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> In at least some cases parks may be closed due to unprovoked tawny owl attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup> The female tends to attack humans from behind and focus on the head and shoulders when physical contact is made, especially if the person turns their back, but even then often veer short of physical contact. Humans are inefficient at fending off sudden physical attacks because of the silent flight of the owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Delmee_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delmee-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hosking_356-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hosking-356">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> Tawny owl mothers not infrequently attack threatening animals common in parks such as dogs and cats as well as <a href="/wiki/Marten" title="Marten">martens</a> (at times knocking them out of the trees) and <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red foxes</a>, especially at dawn or early in the night.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Szomjas_310-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Szomjas-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> Other than scalp abrasions, occular damage can be considerable when tawny owls attack humans.<sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hosking_356-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hosking-356">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps the best-known victim of the tawny owl's fierce attack was the renowned bird photographer <a href="/wiki/Eric_Hosking" title="Eric Hosking">Eric Hosking</a>, who lost his left eye when struck by a bird he was attempting to photograph near its nest in 1937. He later called his autobiography <i>An Eye for a Bird</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hosking2_359-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hosking2-359">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_young">Development of young</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Development of young">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_3young.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Strix_aluco_3young.jpg/220px-Strix_aluco_3young.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Strix_aluco_3young.jpg/330px-Strix_aluco_3young.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Strix_aluco_3young.jpg/440px-Strix_aluco_3young.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2016" data-file-height="1428" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_3young.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The young leave the nest before fledging</div></div></div>
<p>Young begin to call in about 24 hours before they hatch.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_synchrony" title="Reproductive synchrony">Asynchronous hatching</a> occurs but is slightly less pronounced perhaps than long-eared and barn owls, rarely ranging up to 2â€“3 days apart.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The female broods the owlets closely until 10â€“15 days, rarely ceasing as early as 7 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In a Danish study, it was found that 59% of 268 nestlings were male, as opposed to roughly even sex ratio in Great Britain or Hungary, with the ratio not changing annually unlike clutch size, brood size and reproductive success.<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> The gender of broods were studied relatively to <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">testosterone</a> levels in differently sized clutches in Hungary with smaller clutches with lower testosterone levels being male-biased. In this Hungarian study, survival rates were higher in smaller broods than in larger brood. Heavier parents raised all offspring hatched to them, while lighter parents raised 33% of the offspring. 59 of 99 reduced broods were males, while 34 of 81 unmodified broods male.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup> Although sometimes said to only fed young at night,<sup id="cite_ref-Ritter_343-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritter-343">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup> the mother can also parse out tiny bits of food by day to their nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_80-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The young are fed small bits of meat for about 12 days, at which point the young open their eyes and begin to more actively beg.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Raber_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raber-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Also around 12 days, the nestlings produce their first pellets, though they are often of a rather liquid consistency.<sup id="cite_ref-Delmee_156-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delmee-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guerin_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guerin-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Older nestlings beg with quivering wings and intense high-pitched food begging calls.<sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Female only regularly hunts again after brooding period (usually a little after the young are 2 weeks old).<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The male tends not to enter the nest to make food delivery, often the female receives it nearby on a branch of the tree or at the entrance of the nest hole, later when the nestlings are large, the male often will silently deposit the prey directly into the nest without landing.<sup id="cite_ref-Andersen_43-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> When they are 21â€“25 days old, the young are stronger on their legs and feet and begin to spend much time around the entrance of the nest hole. Subsequently, they begin to emerge fully about 3â€“5 days later.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wendland_46-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wendland-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> It was found in warmer conditions, that the owlets born to rufous morph mothers requires less oxygen consumption and may experience lower stress levels during their early development.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> Evidently the oldest is usually the first to leave hole.<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup> After leaving the nest and becoming "branchers", the young owls often clamber around using both the feet and the beak, and often land on the forest floor, from where they tend to flutter and climb into bushes, trying to reach higher parts of the trees (and should not be handled if found on the ground as such).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stulcken_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stulcken-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Delmee_156-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delmee-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Finally, at 29â€“37 days, with an average of 32.1 days in <a href="/wiki/Kielder_Forest" title="Kielder Forest">Kielder Forest</a>, the young <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a>, but take about another two weeks before they can fly strongly.<sup id="cite_ref-Stulcken_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stulcken-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Southern2_197-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern2-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Southern3_364-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern3-364">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Coles_365-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coles-365">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> The young post-fledgling owls continue to beg, often following and rarely leaving an area of about 50&#160;m (160&#160;ft) away from their mother.<sup id="cite_ref-Overskaug2_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overskaug2-366">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> The pre-dispersal young used an area of 5 to 15&#160;ha (12 to 37 acres) in England while, in Scotland, it was only 2.2 to 6.5&#160;ha (5.4 to 16.1 acres).<sup id="cite_ref-Southern3_364-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern3-364">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty6_367-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty6-367">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup> In Denmark, the distance between post-fledging siblings ranged from 11 to 0.6&#160;m (36.1 to 2.0&#160;ft) during day and 32 to 6&#160;m (105 to 20&#160;ft) by night, meaning that they are associative with one another at this stage, and they would spend 20-80% of nighttime hours food begging, up to 82% in poor food years.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde,_P._2016_368-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde,_P._2016-368">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> At around 1 to 3 months (sooner in Denmark, later in England on average), the young tawny owls begin to hunt for themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> At the age of 2 to 3 months the young owls can be evicted by their parents, although often appear to disperse independently.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Southern3_364-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern3-364">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Overskaug2_366-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overskaug2-366">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> However, on average of 72 radio-tagged juveniles in Denmark after the young owls would stop begging for food at 90â€“123 days of age, they would typically roost within their parents territories for another 18 days without incident.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde6_369-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde6-369">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup> In the Danish <a href="/wiki/Gribskov" title="Gribskov">Gribskov forest</a>, 41 radio-tracked broods were dependent after fledging for a mean of 71 days (with a range of 56-84).<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup> 5 of 12 radiotagged juveniles survived dispersal in a different study from Denmark, independence was gained at an average 77.3 days and single day movements recorded of up to 4&#160;km (2.5&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup> 22 radiotagged young tawny owls in England were tracked post-dispersal relative to vole concentrations, though there was no evidence that there were consistently able to access the peak vole areas. 27.4% of area selection was found to be likely correlated to vole access.<sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup> The age at first breeding may be early as one year, but is more commonly 2â€“3 years old and rarely not until 4 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Delmee_156-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delmee-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_success">Breeding success</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Breeding success">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Tawny_Owl_(Strix_aluco)_recently_fledged_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_recently_fledged_%281%29.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_recently_fledged_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_recently_fledged_%281%29.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_recently_fledged_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_recently_fledged_%281%29.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Tawny_Owl_%28Strix_aluco%29_recently_fledged_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="761" data-file-height="605" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Tawny_Owl_(Strix_aluco)_recently_fledged_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny owl of around fledgling age in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Breeding success averages fairly high in this species. A study within oak-hornbeam-birch forest in Hungary on the breeding output of males, 77 males examined from the 1st breeding year until 9 years of age, increase breeding output from 2 to 4 years of age thence peaking at 5â€“6 in age. However, at older than this the male breeding output begins to decline, possibly due to <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">senescence</a>, perhaps since males may be unable to continue withhold high-quality territories from competition. The 5 to 9-year-old males were flexible to prey variations in ways younger males were not.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-373" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-373">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup> In Hungary's <a href="/wiki/Pilis_Mountains" title="Pilis Mountains">Pilis Biosphere Reserve</a> during 1992â€“2000, the age of females appeared to effect the number of eggs and hatching success, while the age of the male effected the number of fledglings, with 39 pairs studied. 98 females and 85 males were produced in a network of 180 nest boxes. Again older males and females (i.e. 5 to 9) were seemingly more productive overall.<sup id="cite_ref-374" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-374">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup> Hungarian data shows lower overall number of young during years with harsh, snowy winters but due to the low number of young, the successful raised young were stronger and in better condition on average once fully grown than the young owls in years with many offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-375" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-375">&#91;375&#93;</a></sup> The breeding success overall in Lithuania has steadily increased, with a survival rate reached of 72% for females.<sup id="cite_ref-376" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-376">&#91;376&#93;</a></sup> The area of <a href="/wiki/Burgundy" title="Burgundy">Burgundy</a> in France, productivity of successful pairs averaged 3.2, varying from 2 to 3 to 4 in poor, intermediate and good prey years.<sup id="cite_ref-377" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-377">&#91;377&#93;</a></sup> In western Switzerland, over the course of 14 years, females produced an average of 5.67 fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-378" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-378">&#91;378&#93;</a></sup> On an area of 3,800&#160;ha (9,400 acres) in the Netherlands, 9 successful breeding attempts were recorded, a very low density to such an extent that unlike many other parts of the range that attempts to attract owls with playback were largely unsuccessful.<sup id="cite_ref-Bos_336-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bos-336">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup> As for breeding success near <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> in Italy, out of 326 attempts, 4-28% were successful in different years, with the number fledglings ranged from 0.4-1 overall and 1â€“1.18 per successful pair and habitats with more rainfall and less arid conditions being more productive.<sup id="cite_ref-379" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-379">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup> Of 311 breeding attempts studied over a 13-year period in Rome, 59.5% failed in urban plots and 51.3% in suburban areas, with 18.5% and 23.4% in urban and suburban zones producing 1 fledgling, 12% and 18% producing 2, 8% and 7.2% producing 3 and 2% in the urban area producing 4. In Roman areas, the breeding rates are generally quite low but are fairly stable annually, due to warmer average ambient temperature and less local trophic competition. The total breeding success in Roman pairs was 43.5% in the city (also 0.83 fledglings per pair and 1.86 per successful pairs) and 51% in suburbs (also 0.82 fledglings per pair, 1.63 per successful pair).<sup id="cite_ref-380" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-380">&#91;380&#93;</a></sup> Of 256 Belgian eggs, 24% did not hatch, 10% of those were deserted, 51% were adled and 39% were deserted or destroyed by the female when food supply was low. In the Belgian data, of 195 young hatched, 94% fledged and 6% died in nest as a result of starvation, with an average number of fledglings 2.06, varying from 0 to 3.25 on average in different years based on vole numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-Delmee_156-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delmee-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In southern France, brood size for all pairs was 2.2 while for successful pair it was 3.2 (range 2â€“4.3 per pair). In the French study, cannibalism was reportedly surprisingly often to be committed by mother or siblings. 73.7% of the studied French broods produced fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-381" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-381">&#91;381&#93;</a></sup> Of 357 pairs in the woodlands around <a href="/wiki/West_Berlin" title="West Berlin">west Berlin</a> in 2 decades starting 1958, 160 pairs produced 333 fledglings, with an average of 2.08 per successful nest. 13 pairs in the city parks of west Berlin produced 47 fledglings, 3.3 pairs per successful nest, productivity strongly correlated to number of yellow-necked mice available.<sup id="cite_ref-382" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-382">&#91;382&#93;</a></sup> More broadly in the Berlin metropolitan, nesting success averaged 2.1 and 2.8 per successful nest, but could vary 2.7 to 3.2 on average in low and high vole years.<sup id="cite_ref-Rockenbauch_330-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockenbauch-330">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> In eastern Bohemia, an average 2.6 young fledged per successful pair, but no determinable difference in productivity was noted during good and poor prey years.<sup id="cite_ref-Plesnik_328-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plesnik-328">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> Breeding success varied in Finland based on vole numbers, from 2.4-2.7 in poor years to 3.4-3.5 in good years, but only a slight variation was recorded in clutch size and young hatched.<sup id="cite_ref-Karhunen_278-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karhunen-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Linkola_331-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Linkola-331">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup> Finland has shown a sharp increase overall in tawny owls in the recent three decades was from 422 to 1710 active territories found, from 198 to 1566 nests found and from 168 to 1341 successful nests found. When vole populations were high, the number of young tawny owls introduced into the population of Finland was ten times higher than in low years.<sup id="cite_ref-383" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-383">&#91;383&#93;</a></sup> It was found in Denmark that a control group of 32 out of 131 radio-tagged young that were supplementally fed by researchers were more vulnerable to predation (36% of these died, mostly due to mammals like foxes around fledging age) but also earlier nests were vulnerable as well (more so to other birds of prey).<sup id="cite_ref-Sunde5_308-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde5-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_Richard_Bartz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Strix_aluco_Richard_Bartz.jpg/220px-Strix_aluco_Richard_Bartz.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Strix_aluco_Richard_Bartz.jpg/330px-Strix_aluco_Richard_Bartz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Strix_aluco_Richard_Bartz.jpg/440px-Strix_aluco_Richard_Bartz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2133" data-file-height="3200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_Richard_Bartz.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A juvenile owl with bits of down still remnant.</div></div></div>
<p>Of 562 eggs laid in Great Britain, 314 young hatched, with 44% lost before hatching and 2% lost before fledging.<sup id="cite_ref-Southern_44-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire" title="Cambridgeshire">Cambridgeshire</a> produced an average of 0.3 for all territories in fragmented woods and 0.89 for all territories in continuous woods.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> This study of the English countryside showed that the owl population varied relatively little in proportion to the sharply cyclic nature of the main prey here, field voles and wood mice, due to the owl's ability to exploit alternate prey in poor rodent years.<sup id="cite_ref-Southern_44-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In Scotland, perhaps with less diverse prey available in the more northern clime, the trends of tawny owls were more sharp: 2.6 fledglings were produced in good vole years, 1.65 average in declining years and 0.2 in poor vole years over a 7-year period.<sup id="cite_ref-Petty7_384-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty7-384">&#91;384&#93;</a></sup> Scottish data showed nestling mortality was higher in poor vole years, at about 31% vs 14% in good vole years, with no sex bias.<sup id="cite_ref-Appleby3_385-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appleby3-385">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup> During low prey years in <a href="/wiki/Wytham" title="Wytham">Wytham</a>, anywhere from 33 to 77% of eggs produced are abandoned and may freeze, but little to no change occurs in territory occupancy.<sup id="cite_ref-Hirons_45-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Authors inferred that the parent owls in <a href="/wiki/Kielder_Forest" title="Kielder Forest">Kielder Forest</a> are able to biological predict the vole numbers based on spring feeding access and produce more often the more productive sex, which were females here.<sup id="cite_ref-Appleby3_385-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appleby3-385">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup> Territorial changes in Kielder were associated to habitat and land use changes with 25% more territories recorded from 1981 to 1991 then a 21% reduction from 1992 to 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_386-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-386">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup> In this area, due probably to the exotic conifer dominated forest, owls were quite dependent on field vole numbers in the openings, and could vary from twentyfold from low to high years, with 70% of the variance of productivity could be correlated to the habitat types available and show the importance of habitat heterogeneity.<sup id="cite_ref-Petty7_384-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty7-384">&#91;384&#93;</a></sup> In peak years at Kielder, of 197 attempts, 6.9% failed to breed and 28.3% successfully bred, in the increase years, of 44 attempts, 10.2% failed to breed and nesting successful was 76.2% and in the decrease years, of 62 attempts, 12.5% failed to breed and nesting success average was 69%.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_386-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-386">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Longevity_and_natural_mortality">Longevity and natural mortality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Longevity and natural mortality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_dead.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Strix_aluco_dead.jpg/220px-Strix_aluco_dead.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Strix_aluco_dead.jpg/330px-Strix_aluco_dead.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Strix_aluco_dead.jpg/440px-Strix_aluco_dead.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco_dead.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A dead tawny owl.</div></div></div>
<p>Tawny owls can live to more than a decade. The oldest recorded in the wild in central Europe was 18 years and 7 months old while the oldest in Sweden recorded was nearly 14 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-Glutz_132-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glutz-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> These records were subsequently broken by a female recorded in the wild in Switzerland at an age of 21 years and 11 months.<sup id="cite_ref-387" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-387">&#91;387&#93;</a></sup> The species has been recorded to live to 27 years or so in captivity.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glutz_132-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glutz-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> If voles are scarce and weather harsh during winter, many tawny owls die by various means (<a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> being primary).<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_10-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Starvation rates are high in newly independent young if there is no unoccupied territories in the vicinity (at least by 1 bird of their corresponding gender).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Mortality averaged about 15% in territorial adults in Wytham.<sup id="cite_ref-Hirons_45-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirons-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In Finland, Ural owl displaced tawny owls but great grey owl peaceably allow them in the vicinity.<sup id="cite_ref-Lahti_388-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lahti-388">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup> Although moderately hardy during sub-freezing winters, severe winters can be dangerous in areas such as the Russian part of their range.<sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_110-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glutz_132-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glutz-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Mortality in general in the northern limits of the range is probably higher in the more temperate zone adapted tawny than it is in the Ural owl.<sup id="cite_ref-389" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-389">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup> This species is increasingly affected by <a href="/wiki/Avian_malaria" title="Avian malaria">avian malaria</a>, the incidence of which has tripled in the last 70 years, in parallel with increasing global temperatures. An increase of one degree Celsius produces a two- to three-fold increase in the rate of malaria. In 2010, the incidence in British tawny owls was 60%, compared to 2â€“3% in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-GaramszegI_390-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GaramszegI-390">&#91;390&#93;</a></sup> Direct <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a> causes of mortality are covered later. Of 22 radio-tagged young tawny owls in Kielder, 36.4% (8) owls died 10â€“106 days after fledgling but while still on parent's ranges, another 22.7% (5) died after leaving parents territory at 40â€“147 days after fledgling, 22.7% (5) also disappeared after fledged but while still dependent and were quite likely preyed upon while the only 4 remaining lost radio contact after leaving parent's range at 58â€“178 days after fledging. Of 13 certain to have died, 6 were due to starvation, 3 were due to predation by goshawks, one by an infected eye injury, while the rest of uncertain cause.<sup id="cite_ref-Coles_365-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coles-365">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> Mortality rates can be even higher elsewhere such as an average of about 92% in Scotland and about 61%  in Norway for juveniles before they disperse.<sup id="cite_ref-Petty6_367-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty6-367">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Overskaug2_366-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overskaug2-366">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, recorded average mortality rates in the 1st year of radio-tagged tawny owl lives were recorded as the following averages: Sweden at 71.7%, Switzerland at 49.4%, England at 52.6%, west Germany at 48% and Belgium at 58%. Radiotagging studies showed average mortality for Swedish 2nd year owls was 44.3%, 24.5% at this point for Swiss owls and 22.2% for English ones while in the third year the Swedish one's mortality was estimated to average 47.6% and English ones at 31.8%.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The survival rate average over two decades was unexpectedly somewhat high in Finland in about 20,000 owls, where it was 33% in the 1st year, 64% in 2nd year, 73% in subsequent years.<sup id="cite_ref-391" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-391">&#91;391&#93;</a></sup> In England, it was estimated that there was 6-11 times lower survival rate for tawny owls that were <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_radio_telemetry" title="Wildlife radio telemetry">ragiotagged</a> than for those that were not based on estimates, the theory as to why it lower survival is that the additional weight burden of the radio-tags themselves may have inhibited capture of food and also made juveniles more vulnerable to goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-392" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-392">&#91;392&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasitism_and_bacterial_infections">Parasitism and bacterial infections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Parasitism and bacterial infections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Parasitic_worm" title="Parasitic worm">Helminths</a> are also fairly common and may compromise the condition of many owls, if rarely the cause of direct fatalities.<sup id="cite_ref-393" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-393">&#91;393&#93;</a></sup> Parasites commonly recorded in tawny owls including <i><a href="/wiki/Leucocytozoon" title="Leucocytozoon">Leucocytozoon</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Haemoproteus" title="Haemoproteus">Haemoproteus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Toxoplasma" class="mw-redirect" title="Toxoplasma">Toxoplasma</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Trypanosoma" title="Trypanosoma">Trypanosoma</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Centrorhynchus" class="mw-redirect" title="Centrorhynchus">Centrorhynchus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tetrameridae" title="Tetrameridae">Tetrameres</a></i>, with different species of <i>Leucocytozoon</i> present in about 95% of 32 individuals in Great Britain and  57% of study specimens in Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-394" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-394">&#91;394&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-395" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-395">&#91;395&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-396" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-396">&#91;396&#93;</a></sup> <i>Haemoptroteus</i> was the prevalent parasites in France and also quite prominent in birds from Turkey.<sup id="cite_ref-397" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-397">&#91;397&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-398" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-398">&#91;398&#93;</a></sup> The first records of <i><a href="/wiki/Trichinella" title="Trichinella">Trichinella</a></i> were recorded recently in Europe as well as the similarly rare <i><a href="/wiki/Eimeria" title="Eimeria">Eimeria</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-399"
