class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-399">&#91;399&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-400" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-400">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> Blood parasitism was more common in England when food supplies were lowered.<sup id="cite_ref-401" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-401">&#91;401&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-402" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-402">&#91;402&#93;</a></sup> In some cases, parasites are contracted directly from their prey.<sup id="cite_ref-403" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-403">&#91;403&#93;</a></sup> It was estimated that about 25% of free-living adults in England had some variety of <a href="/wiki/Eye_injury" title="Eye injury">ocular lesions</a>, which may lead to cases of <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterial</a> infections and <a href="/wiki/Conjunctiva" title="Conjunctiva">conjunctiva</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-404" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-404">&#91;404&#93;</a></sup> Concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Enterobacteriaceae" title="Enterobacteriaceae">Enterobacteriaceae</a>, which can be fatal to humans, are sometimes found in nest boxes.<sup id="cite_ref-405" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-405">&#91;405&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rosenthal_protestant_church_cemetery_berlin,_closeup_of_tawny_owl.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Rosenthal_protestant_church_cemetery_berlin%2C_closeup_of_tawny_owl.png/220px-Rosenthal_protestant_church_cemetery_berlin%2C_closeup_of_tawny_owl.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Rosenthal_protestant_church_cemetery_berlin%2C_closeup_of_tawny_owl.png/330px-Rosenthal_protestant_church_cemetery_berlin%2C_closeup_of_tawny_owl.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Rosenthal_protestant_church_cemetery_berlin%2C_closeup_of_tawny_owl.png/440px-Rosenthal_protestant_church_cemetery_berlin%2C_closeup_of_tawny_owl.png 2x" data-file-width="2270" data-file-height="2037" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rosenthal_protestant_church_cemetery_berlin,_closeup_of_tawny_owl.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny owl makes use of a steeple as a roost in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny owl is a common bird, especially in central Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> A mid-1990s estimate of the European population was 400,000-800,000 pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The tawny owl has a geographical range of at least 10&#160;million&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.8&#160;million&#160;mi<sup>2</sup>) and a large population including an estimated 970,000â€“2,000,000 individuals in Europe alone with more extensive surveys two decades later.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty9_406-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty9-406">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup> Population trends have not been quantified, but there is evidence of an overall increase. This owl is not believed to meet the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a> criterion of declining more than 30% in ten years or three generations and is therefore evaluated as being of <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty9_406-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty9-406">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup> Globally, the population may be estimated anywhere from 1 million to nearly 3 million individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> 50% of European numbers were in the nations of <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty9_406-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty9-406">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup> In the UK it is on the <a href="/wiki/RSPB" class="mw-redirect" title="RSPB">RSPB</a> Amber List of Concern.<sup id="cite_ref-RSPB2_407-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSPB2-407">&#91;407&#93;</a></sup> In Great Britain, estimated number there in the 1970s ran up to 100,000 pairs whereas more recent estimate suggest much fewer, at about 20,000 pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Petty9_406-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty9-406">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup> Although estimates are variable, close study has shown a population reduction of fairly nebulous cause in Britain of around 11% from 1968-1972 to 1988-1991 or of an estimated 6% from 1970 to 2010, utilizing two different surveying methods.<sup id="cite_ref-408" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-408">&#91;408&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-409" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-409">&#91;409&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-410" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-410">&#91;410&#93;</a></sup> Relative to the loss of other British woodland birds, which has been pronounced, the tawny owl reductions may be considered small to moderate. Among myriad and complex causes for all forest birds in Britain, for the tawny owl it is mentioned that competition for nest sites with introduced <a href="/wiki/Eastern_gray_squirrel" title="Eastern gray squirrel">eastern gray squirrels</a> (<i>Sciurus carolinensis</i>) may be responsible for some of the reductions.<sup id="cite_ref-411" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-411">&#91;411&#93;</a></sup> The French population of tawny owls is very large and increased in estimated numbers from 10,000-100,000 in the early 1970s to 50,000-150,000 estimated in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> 5 digit numbers are estimated in many European countries, including Germany, Italy, Poland, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It is thought that declines may have occurred in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> and even Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-Snow_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The estimated number of breeding pairs within Denmark using different calculation methods was very variable with different estimates from 4800 to 7400 to 20,500.<sup id="cite_ref-412" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-412">&#91;412&#93;</a></sup> A large number were estimated in Turkey, perhaps up to an estimated 25,000 pairs, with Syria estimated up to 50 pairs and 250 pairs roughly in Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> While tawny owls may commonly use nest boxes, these are not considered necessary given the general stability of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Increased populations pose a risk to smaller owls in areas of sympatry and may hamper conservation efforts of such species.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig2_315-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig2-315">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides natural causes such as predation and starvation, collision with vehicles, power lines, any other kind of wire collision and other manmade objects is a regular cause of tawny owl mortality.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-413" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-413">&#91;413&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, the species is exposed to assorted forms of human <a href="/wiki/Persecution" title="Persecution">persecution</a> (from egg-collecting to shootings and poisonings) but it is not nearly as extensively persecuted as larger birds like golden eagles, goshawks and eagle owls. However, earlier in the 20th century, as many as 12% of birds recovered in <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandivania&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Scandivania (page does not exist)">Scandivania</a> were dead from various forms of persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_6-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Olsson_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-414" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-414">&#91;414&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-415" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-415">&#91;415&#93;</a></sup> Although they are fairly adaptable to <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a>, there are limits to the tawny owls adaptability, especially due to declines in woodland quality, prey access and human-sourced mortality sources, one of the worst of which is collision with vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_7-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-416" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-416">&#91;416&#93;</a></sup> Of 112 tawny owls <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_rehabilitation" title="Wildlife rehabilitation">re-released</a> in the wild from England's <a href="/wiki/East_Winch_Common" title="East Winch Common">East Winch Wildlife Centre</a>, 17% were subsequently recovered (about half of which were dead), with the most diagnosed cause of mishap being road accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-417" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-417">&#91;417&#93;</a></sup> Of 57 tawny owls released in <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hampshire" title="Hampshire">Hampshire</a>, 28% were recovered dead.<sup id="cite_ref-418" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-418">&#91;418&#93;</a></sup> However, tawny owls, perhaps due to their liking of wooded rather open areas, are relatively less vulnerable to automobile deaths than some other owls, i.e. of hundreds dead owls on roadsides in Italy, only 4.5% were tawnys, while 41% were little owls and 39% barn owls, while in Germany, 7.4% were tawnys, against 53% barn owls and 28.4% little owls.<sup id="cite_ref-419" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-419">&#91;419&#93;</a></sup> Variable levels of occular damage, with comparable levels of ocular trauma to humans who are victims of car collisions, were detected in 50 tawny owls in England recovered after surviving car collisions.<sup id="cite_ref-420" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-420">&#91;420&#93;</a></sup> Damage to the occular area was recorded in 128 of 216 free-living tawnys in southwest England, and in 60.5% of 147 admitted birds the injury were due to road accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-421" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-421">&#91;421&#93;</a></sup> In the forest of <a href="/wiki/Burgundy" title="Burgundy">Burgundy</a>, road-based deaths were the main cause of mortality that could be diagnosed.<sup id="cite_ref-422" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-422">&#91;422&#93;</a></sup> Roadkills of tawny owls were fairly frequent in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, but were not usually in prime habitat and often occurred near the borders of existing territories.<sup id="cite_ref-423" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-423">&#91;423&#93;</a></sup> However, density was reduced by road development in Portuguese owls, largely due to the fragmentation of habitat, disturbance and  noise pollution that may effect intraspecific communication and hunting and increase mortality may come with major roads for owls.<sup id="cite_ref-424" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-424">&#91;424&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in Germany, despite the general adaptability to development in tawny owls, locally the noise pollution and increased fragmentation of woods was found to be reducing tawny owl populations.<sup id="cite_ref-425" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-425">&#91;425&#93;</a></sup> An unclear number of tawny owls may be killed by <a href="/wiki/Toxic_heavy_metal" title="Toxic heavy metal">heavy metal contamination</a> via <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulation</a> as well as via <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a> use.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-426" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-426">&#91;426&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">Lead</a> levels were difficult to estimate in Spanish owls due to the large number of feathers needed to estimate them, while dead Spanish owls had middling, variable levels of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> concentrations.<sup id="cite_ref-427" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-427">&#91;427&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-428" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-428">&#91;428&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-429" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-429">&#91;429&#93;</a></sup> In eggs from Norway, <a href="/wiki/Per-_and_polyfluoroalkyl_substances" title="Per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances">polyfluoroalkyl compounds</a> were studied, in sum toxicological implications were low and several times lower than would be implied by the diversity of compounds found in the eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-430" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-430">&#91;430&#93;</a></sup> British birds appear to be effected by second-generation <a href="/wiki/Anticoagulants" class="mw-redirect" title="Anticoagulants">anticoagulants</a>, with about 20% of 192 birds showing detectable residues of these. The number is lower than other raptors analyzed here such as <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">red kite</a> and the number of tawnys did not perceptibly decline due to this.<sup id="cite_ref-431" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-431">&#91;431&#93;</a></sup> In Sweden and perhaps Germany, the level of <a href="/wiki/Per-_and_polyfluoroalkyl_substances" title="Per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances">PFAS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> were fairly low, comparable to common kestrel and much lower than the levels in <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">ospreys</a> (<i>Pandion haliaetus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-432" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-432">&#91;432&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sunde,_P._2016_368-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunde,_P._2016-368">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-433" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-433">&#91;433&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Strix_aluco.jpg/220px-Strix_aluco.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Strix_aluco.jpg/330px-Strix_aluco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Strix_aluco.jpg/440px-Strix_aluco.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1772" data-file-height="1181" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strix_aluco.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A grey individual, probably subspecies <i>S. a. aluco</i></div></div></div>
<p>The tawny owl, like its relatives, has often been seen as an omen of bad luck; <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> used it as such in <i><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(play)" title="Julius Caesar (play)">Julius Caesar</a></i> (Act 1 Scene 3): "And yesterday the bird of night did sit/ Even at noon-day upon the market-place/ Hooting and shrieking." <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a> is quoted as saying "Whatever wise people may say of them, I at least have found the owl's cry always prophetic of mischief to me".<sup id="cite_ref-Armstrong_434-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-434">&#91;434&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wordsworth described the technique for calling an owl in his poem <i>There was a Boy</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wordsworth_435-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wordsworth-435">&#91;435&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><div class="poem">
<p>And there, with fingers interwoven, both hands<br />
Pressed closely palm to palm and to his mouth<br />
Uplifted, he, as through an instrument,<br />
Blew mimic hootings to the silent owls,<br />
That they might answer him.â€”And they would shout<br />
Across the watery vale, and shout again,<br />
Responsive to his call,â€”with quivering peals,<br />
And long halloos, and screams, and echoes loud<br />
Redoubled and redoubled; concourse wild<br />
Of jocund din!
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-Syrnium-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Syrnium_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For <i>Syrnium aluco</i> see for instance: <cite id="CITEREFPowys,_Baron_LilfordSalvinNewtonKeulemans1885" class="citation book cs1"><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Powys,_4th_Baron_Lilford" title="Thomas Powys, 4th Baron Lilford">Powys, Baron Lilford, Thomas Littleton</a>; <a href="/wiki/Osbert_Salvin" title="Osbert Salvin">Salvin, Osbert</a>; <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Newton" title="Alfred Newton">Newton, Alfred</a>; <a href="/wiki/John_Gerrard_Keulemans" title="John Gerrard Keulemans">Keulemans, John Gerrard</a> (1885). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biodiversitylibrary.org/page/34514947"><i>Coloured figures of the birds of the British Islands</i></a>. <b>1</b>. <li id="cite_note-Sclater-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Sclater_3-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sclater, P. L. (1879). <i>Remarks on the Nomenclature of the British Owls, and on the Arrangement of the Order Striges</i>. Ibis, 21(3), 346-352.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Konig-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Konig_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Konig_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Konig_4-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Konig_4-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Konig_4-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Konig_4-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Konig_4-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Konig_4-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a
